       <tr>
        <td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
         <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
          <ul>
           <li class="nv-view">
            <a href="/wiki/Template:Electromagnetism" title="Template:Electromagnetism">
             <abbr title="View this template">
              v
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li class="nv-talk">
            <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Electromagnetism" title="Template talk:Electromagnetism">
             <abbr title="Discuss this template">
              t
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li class="nv-edit">
            <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Electromagnetism&amp;action=edit">
             <abbr title="Edit this template">
              e
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
          </ul>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       A
       <b>
        magnetic field
       </b>
       is a
       <a href="/wiki/Force_field_(physics)" title="Force field (physics)">
        force field
       </a>
       that is created by moving
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">
        electric charges
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">
        electric currents
       </a>
       ) and
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_dipole" title="Magnetic dipole">
        magnetic dipoles
       </a>
       , and exerts a force on other nearby moving charges and magnetic dipoles. At any given point, it has a
       <i>
        direction
       </i>
       and a
       <i>
        magnitude
       </i>
       (or strength), so it is represented by a
       <a href="/wiki/Vector_field" title="Vector field">
        vector field
       </a>
       . The term is used for two distinct but closely related fields denoted by the symbols
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       and
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       , where, in the
       <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">
        International System of Units
       </a>
       ,
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       is measured in units of
       <a href="/wiki/Ampere" title="Ampere">
        amperes
       </a>
       per
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meter" title="Meter">
        meter
       </a>
       and
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       is measured in
       <a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">
        teslas
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">
        newtons
       </a>
       per meter per ampere. In a
       <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">
        vacuum
       </a>
       ,
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       and
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       are the same aside from units; but in a material with a
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetization" title="Magnetization">
        magnetization
       </a>
       (denoted by the symbol
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         M
        </b>
       </span>
       ),
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       is
       <a href="/wiki/Solenoidal_vector_field" title="Solenoidal vector field">
        solenoidal
       </a>
       (having no
       <a href="/wiki/Divergence" title="Divergence">
        divergence
       </a>
       in its spatial dependence) while
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       is
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irrotational_vector_field" title="Irrotational vector field">
        irrotational
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Curl_(mathematics)" title="Curl (mathematics)">
        curl
       </a>
       -free).
      </p>
      <p>
       Magnetic fields can be produced by moving
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">
        electric charges
       </a>
       and the intrinsic
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_moment" title="Magnetic moment">
        magnetic moments
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" title="Elementary particle">
        elementary particles
       </a>
       associated with a fundamental
       <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">
        quantum property
       </a>
       , their
       <a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">
        spin
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jiles_1-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jiles-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feynman_2-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Feynman-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Magnetic fields and
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">
        electric fields
       </a>
       are interrelated, and are both components of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_force" title="Electromagnetic force">
        electromagnetic force
       </a>
       , one of the four
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fundamental_force" title="Fundamental force">
        fundamental forces
       </a>
       of nature.
      </p>
      <p>
       In everyday life, magnetic fields are most often encountered as a
       <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">
        force
       </a>
       created by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Permanent_magnet" title="Permanent magnet">
        permanent magnets
       </a>
       , which pull on
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferromagnetic_material" title="Ferromagnetic material">
        ferromagnetic materials
       </a>
       such as iron, cobalt, or nickel, and attract or repel other
       <a href="/wiki/Magnet" title="Magnet">
        magnets
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Magnetic fields are widely used throughout modern technology, particularly in
       <a href="/wiki/Electrical_engineering" title="Electrical engineering">
        electrical engineering
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Electromechanics" title="Electromechanics">
        electromechanics
       </a>
       . The Earth produces
       <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth's magnetic field">
        its own magnetic field
       </a>
       , which shields the Earth's ozone layer from the
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">
        solar wind
       </a>
       and is important in
       <a href="/wiki/Navigation" title="Navigation">
        navigation
       </a>
       using a
       <a href="/wiki/Compass" title="Compass">
        compass
       </a>
       . Rotating magnetic fields are used in both
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor" title="Electric motor">
        electric motors
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_generator" title="Electric generator">
        generators
       </a>
       . Magnetic forces give information about the charge carriers in a material through the
       <a href="/wiki/Hall_effect" title="Hall effect">
        Hall effect
       </a>
       . The interaction of magnetic fields in electric devices such as transformers is studied in the discipline of
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_circuit" title="Magnetic circuit">
        magnetic circuits
       </a>
       .
      </p>

       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Descartes_magnetic_field.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1165" data-file-width="1372" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Descartes_magnetic_field.jpg/300px-Descartes_magnetic_field.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Descartes_magnetic_field.jpg/450px-Descartes_magnetic_field.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Descartes_magnetic_field.jpg/600px-Descartes_magnetic_field.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Descartes_magnetic_field.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         One of the first drawings of a magnetic field, by
         <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">
          René Descartes
         </a>
         , 1644, showing the Earth attracting lodestones. It illustrated his theory that magnetism was caused by the circulation of tiny helical particles, "threaded parts", through threaded pores in magnets.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Although magnets and magnetism were known much earlier, the study of magnetic fields began in 1269 when French scholar
       <a href="/wiki/Petrus_Peregrinus_de_Maricourt" title="Petrus Peregrinus de Maricourt">
        Petrus Peregrinus de Maricourt
       </a>
       mapped out the magnetic field on the surface of a spherical magnet using iron needles.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [nb 1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Noting that the resulting field lines crossed at two points he named those points 'poles' in analogy to Earth's poles. He also clearly articulated the principle that magnets always have both a north and south pole, no matter how finely one slices them.
      </p>
      <p>
       Almost three centuries later,
       <a href="/wiki/William_Gilbert_(astronomer)" title="William Gilbert (astronomer)">
        William Gilbert
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Colchester" title="Colchester">
        Colchester
       </a>
       replicated Petrus Peregrinus' work and was the first to state explicitly that Earth is a magnet.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Published in 1600, Gilbert's work,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/De_Magnete" title="De Magnete">
         De Magnete
        </a>
       </i>
       , helped to establish magnetism as a science.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1750,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Michell" title="John Michell">
        John Michell
       </a>
       stated that magnetic poles attract and repel in accordance with an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inverse_square_law" title="Inverse square law">
        inverse square law
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Charles-Augustin_de_Coulomb" title="Charles-Augustin de Coulomb">
        Charles-Augustin de Coulomb
       </a>
       experimentally verified this in 1785 and stated explicitly that the north and south poles cannot be separated.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Building on this force between poles,
       <a href="/wiki/Sim%C3%A9on_Denis_Poisson" title="Siméon Denis Poisson">
        Siméon Denis Poisson
       </a>
       (1781–1840) created the first successful model of the magnetic field, which he presented in 1824.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In this model, a magnetic
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       -field is produced by 'magnetic poles' and magnetism is due to small pairs of north/south magnetic poles.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hans_Christian_%C3%98rsted,_Der_Geist_in_der_Natur,_1854.tiff">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2968" data-file-width="1936" height="337" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Hans_Christian_%C3%98rsted%2C_Der_Geist_in_der_Natur%2C_1854.tiff/lossy-page1-220px-Hans_Christian_%C3%98rsted%2C_Der_Geist_in_der_Natur%2C_1854.tiff.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Hans_Christian_%C3%98rsted%2C_Der_Geist_in_der_Natur%2C_1854.tiff/lossy-page1-330px-Hans_Christian_%C3%98rsted%2C_Der_Geist_in_der_Natur%2C_1854.tiff.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Hans_Christian_%C3%98rsted%2C_Der_Geist_in_der_Natur%2C_1854.tiff/lossy-page1-440px-Hans_Christian_%C3%98rsted%2C_Der_Geist_in_der_Natur%2C_1854.tiff.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hans_Christian_%C3%98rsted,_Der_Geist_in_der_Natur,_1854.tiff" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Hans_Christian_%C3%98rsted" title="Hans Christian Ørsted">
          Hans Christian Ørsted
         </a>
         ,
         <i>
          Der Geist in der Natur
         </i>
         , 1854
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Three discoveries challenged this foundation of magnetism, though. First, in 1819,
       <a href="/wiki/Hans_Christian_%C3%98rsted" title="Hans Christian Ørsted">
        Hans Christian Ørsted
       </a>
       discovered that an electric current generates a magnetic field encircling it. Then in 1820,
       <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9-Marie_Amp%C3%A8re" title="André-Marie Ampère">
        André-Marie Ampère
       </a>
       showed that parallel wires having currents in the same direction repel one another. Finally,
       <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Biot" title="Jean-Baptiste Biot">
        Jean-Baptiste Biot
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A9lix_Savart" title="Félix Savart">
        Félix Savart
       </a>
       discovered the
       <a href="/wiki/Biot%E2%80%93Savart_law" title="Biot–Savart law">
        Biot–Savart law
       </a>
       in 1820, which correctly predicts the magnetic field around any current-carrying wire.
      </p>
      <p>
       Extending these experiments, Ampère published his own successful model of magnetism in 1825. In it, he showed the equivalence of electrical currents to magnets
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and proposed that magnetism is due to perpetually flowing loops of current instead of the dipoles of magnetic charge in Poisson's model.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [nb 2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This has the additional benefit of explaining why magnetic charge can not be isolated. Further, Ampère derived both
       <a href="/wiki/Amp%C3%A8re%27s_force_law" title="Ampère's force law">
        Ampère's force law
       </a>
       describing the force between two currents and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amp%C3%A8re%27s_law" title="Ampère's law">
        Ampère's law
       </a>
       , which, like the Biot–Savart law, correctly described the magnetic field generated by a steady current. Also in this work, Ampère introduced the term
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrodynamics" title="Electrodynamics">
        electrodynamics
       </a>
       to describe the relationship between electricity and magnetism.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1831,
       <a href="/wiki/Michael_Faraday" title="Michael Faraday">
        Michael Faraday
       </a>
       discovered electromagnetic induction when he found that a changing magnetic field generates an encircling electric field. He described this phenomenon in what is known as
       <a href="/wiki/Faraday%27s_law_of_induction" title="Faraday's law of induction">
        Faraday's law of induction
       </a>
       . Later,
       <a href="/wiki/Franz_Ernst_Neumann" title="Franz Ernst Neumann">
        Franz Ernst Neumann
       </a>
       proved that, for a moving conductor in a magnetic field, induction is a consequence of Ampère's force law.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the process, he introduced the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_vector_potential" title="Magnetic vector potential">
        magnetic vector potential
       </a>
       , which was later shown to be equivalent to the underlying mechanism proposed by Faraday.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1850,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lord_Kelvin" title="Lord Kelvin">
        Lord Kelvin
       </a>
       , then known as William Thomson, distinguished between two magnetic fields now denoted
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       and
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       . The former applied to Poisson's model and the latter to Ampère's model and induction.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Further, he derived how
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       and
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       relate to each other.
      </p>
      <p>
       The reason
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       and
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       are used for the two magnetic fields has been a source of some debate among science historians. Most agree that Kelvin avoided
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         M
        </b>
       </span>
       to prevent confusion with the SI fundamental unit of length, the
       <a href="/wiki/Metre" title="Metre">
        Metre
       </a>
       , abbreviated "m". Others believe the choices were purely random.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Between 1861 and 1865,
       <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">
        James Clerk Maxwell
       </a>
       developed and published
       <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%27s_equations" title="Maxwell's equations">
        Maxwell's equations
       </a>
       , which explained and united all of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_theory" title="Classical theory">
        classical
       </a>
       electricity and magnetism. The first set of these equations was published in a paper entitled
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:On_Physical_Lines_of_Force.pdf" title="commons:File:On Physical Lines of Force.pdf">
         On Physical Lines of Force
        </a>
       </i>
       in 1861. These equations were valid although incomplete. Maxwell completed his set of equations in his later 1865 paper
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/A_Dynamical_Theory_of_the_Electromagnetic_Field" title="A Dynamical Theory of the Electromagnetic Field">
         A Dynamical Theory of the Electromagnetic Field
        </a>
       </i>
       and demonstrated the fact that light is an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_wave" title="Electromagnetic wave">
        electromagnetic wave
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Hertz" title="Heinrich Hertz">
        Heinrich Hertz
       </a>
       experimentally confirmed this fact in 1887.
      </p>
      <p>
       The twentieth century extended electrodynamics to include relativity and quantum mechanics.
       <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">
        Albert Einstein
       </a>
       , in his paper of 1905 that established relativity, showed that both the electric and magnetic fields are part of the same phenomena viewed from different reference frames. (See
       <a href="/wiki/Moving_magnet_and_conductor_problem" title="Moving magnet and conductor problem">
        moving magnet and conductor problem
       </a>
       for details about the
       <a href="/wiki/Thought_experiment" title="Thought experiment">
        thought experiment
       </a>
       that eventually helped Albert Einstein to develop
       <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">
        special relativity
       </a>
       .) Finally, the emergent field of
       <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">
        quantum mechanics
       </a>
       was merged with electrodynamics to form
       <a href="/wiki/Quantum_electrodynamics" title="Quantum electrodynamics">
        quantum electrodynamics
       </a>
       (QED).
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span id="Definitions.2C_units.2C_and_measurement">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions,_units,_and_measurement">
        Definitions, units, and measurement
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Definitions, units, and measurement">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="The_B-field">
        The B-field
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: The B-field">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <table class="wikitable" style="float:right;">
       <tr>
        <th>
         Alternative names for
         <b>
          B
         </b>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Electromagnetics_14-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-Electromagnetics-14">
           [12]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <ul>
          <li>
           Magnetic flux density
          </li>
          <li>
           Magnetic field
          </li>
         </ul>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Alternative names for
         <b>
          H
         </b>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Electromagnetics_14-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-Electromagnetics-14">
           [12]
          </a>
         </sup>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
          <a href="#cite_note-15">
           [13]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <ul>
          <li>
           Magnetic field intensity
          </li>
          <li>
           Magnetic field strength
          </li>
          <li>
           Magnetic field
          </li>
          <li>
           Magnetizing field
          </li>
         </ul>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       The magnetic field can be defined in several equivalent ways based on the effects it has on its environment.
      </p>
      <p>
       Often the magnetic field is defined by the force it exerts on a moving charged particle. It is known from experiments in
       <a href="/wiki/Electrostatics" title="Electrostatics">
        electrostatics
       </a>
       that a particle of charge
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         q
        </i>
       </span>
       in an electric field
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         E
        </b>
       </span>
       experiences a force
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         F
        </b>
        =
        <i>
         q
        </i>
        <b>
         E
        </b>
       </span>
       . However, in other situations, such as when a charged particle moves in the vicinity of a current-carrying wire, the force also depends on the velocity of that particle. Fortunately, the velocity dependent portion can be separated out such that the force on the particle satisfies the
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lorentz_force_law" title="Lorentz force law">
         Lorentz force law
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {F} =q(\mathbf {E} +\mathbf {v} \times \mathbf {B} ).}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                F
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mi>
               q
              </mi>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               (
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                E
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               +
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                v
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ×
               <!-- × -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                B
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               )
              </mo>
              <mo>
               .
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle \mathbf {F} =q(\mathbf {E} +\mathbf {v} \times \mathbf {B} ).}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="\mathbf {F} =q(\mathbf {E} +\mathbf {v} \times \mathbf {B} )." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/98b985e6332ac21f2eb2b6aeefae9135fe6fe3c6" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:19.057ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Here
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         v
        </b>
       </span>
       is the particle's velocity and × denotes the
       <a href="/wiki/Cross_product" title="Cross product">
        cross product
       </a>
       . The vector
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       is termed the magnetic field, and it is
       <i>
        defined
       </i>
       as the vector field necessary to make the Lorentz force law correctly describe the motion of a charged particle. This definition allows the determination of
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       in the following way
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-purcell_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-purcell-16">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        [T]he command, "Measure the direction and magnitude of the vector
        <span class="texhtml">
         <b>
          B
         </b>
        </span>
        at such and such a place," calls for the following operations: Take a particle of known charge
        <span class="texhtml">
         <i>
          q
         </i>
        </span>
        . Measure the force on
        <span class="texhtml">
         <i>
          q
         </i>
        </span>
        at rest, to determine
        <span class="texhtml">
         <b>
          E
         </b>
        </span>
        . Then measure the force on the particle when its velocity is
        <span class="texhtml">
         <b>
          v
         </b>
        </span>
        ; repeat with
        <span class="texhtml">
         <b>
          v
         </b>
        </span>
        in some other direction. Now find a
        <span class="texhtml">
         <b>
          B
         </b>
        </span>
        that makes the Lorentz force law fit all these results—that is the magnetic field at the place in question.
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       Alternatively, the magnetic field can be defined in terms of the
       <a href="/wiki/Torque" title="Torque">
        torque
       </a>
       it produces on a magnetic dipole (see
       <a href="#Magnetic_torque_on_permanent_magnets">
        magnetic torque on permanent magnets
       </a>
       below).
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="The_H-field">
        The H-field
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: The H-field">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In addition to
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       , there is a quantity
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       , which is also sometimes called the
       <i>
        magnetic field
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [nb 3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In a vacuum,
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       and
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       are proportional to each other, with the multiplicative constant depending on the physical units. Inside a material they are different (see
       <a href="#H-field_and_magnetic_materials">
        H and B inside and outside magnetic materials
       </a>
       ). The term "magnetic field" is historically reserved for
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       while using other terms for
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       . Informally, though, and formally for some recent textbooks mostly in physics, the term 'magnetic field' is used to describe
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       as well as or in place of
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ex03_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ex03-18">
         [nb 4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There are many alternative names for both (see sidebar).
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Units">
        Units
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Units">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SI" title="SI">
        SI
       </a>
       units,
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       is measured in
       <a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">
        teslas
       </a>
       (symbol: T) and correspondingly
       <span class="texhtml">
        Φ
        <sub>
         <i>
          B
         </i>
        </sub>
       </span>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_flux" title="Magnetic flux">
        magnetic flux
       </a>
       ) is measured in
       <a href="/wiki/Weber_(unit)" title="Weber (unit)">
        webers
       </a>
       (symbol: Wb) so that a flux density of 1 Wb/m
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       is 1
       <a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">
        tesla
       </a>
       . The SI unit of tesla is equivalent to (
       <a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">
        newton
       </a>
       ·
       <a href="/wiki/Second" title="Second">
        second
       </a>
       )/(
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coulomb_(unit)" title="Coulomb (unit)">
        coulomb
       </a>
       ·
       <a href="/wiki/Metre" title="Metre">
        metre
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [nb 5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Gaussian_units" title="Gaussian units">
        Gaussian-cgs units
       </a>
       ,
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       is measured in
       <a href="/wiki/Gauss_(unit)" title="Gauss (unit)">
        gauss
       </a>
       (symbol: G). (The conversion is 1 T = 10,000 G.) One nanotesla is also called a gamma (symbol: γ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NGDC_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NGDC-20">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       -field is measured in
       <a href="/wiki/Ampere" title="Ampere">
        amperes
       </a>
       per metre (A/m) in SI units, and in
       <a href="/wiki/Oersted" title="Oersted">
        oersteds
       </a>
       (Oe) in cgs units.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Measurement">
        Measurement
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Measurement">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The precision attained for a magnetic field measurement for Gravity Probe B experiment is 5 attoteslas (
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         6982500000000000000♠
        </span>
        5
        <span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">
         ×
        </span>
        10
        <sup>
         −18
        </sup>
        T
       </span>
       );
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the largest magnetic field produced in a laboratory is 2.8 kT (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/VNIIEF" title="VNIIEF">
        VNIIEF
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Sarov" title="Sarov">
        Sarov
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">
        Russia
       </a>
       , 1998).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The magnetic field of some astronomical objects such as
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetar" title="Magnetar">
        magnetars
       </a>
       are much higher; magnetars range from 0.1 to 100 GT (10
       <sup>
        8
       </sup>
       to 10
       <sup>
        11
       </sup>
       T).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       See
       <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude_(magnetic_field)" title="Orders of magnitude (magnetic field)">
        orders of magnitude (magnetic field)
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Devices used to measure the local magnetic field are called
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetometer" title="Magnetometer">
        magnetometers
       </a>
       . Important classes of magnetometers include using
       <a href="/wiki/Search_coil" title="Search coil">
        induction magnetometer
       </a>
       (or search-coil magnetometer) which measure only varying magnetic field,
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetometer#Rotating_coil_magnetometer" title="Magnetometer">
        rotating coil magnetometer
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hall_effect" title="Hall effect">
        Hall effect
       </a>
       magnetometers,
       <a href="/wiki/Proton_magnetometer" title="Proton magnetometer">
        NMR magnetometers
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/SQUID" title="SQUID">
        SQUID magnetometers
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetometer#Fluxgate_magnetometer" title="Magnetometer">
        fluxgate magnetometers
       </a>
       . The magnetic fields of distant
       <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_object" title="Astronomical object">
        astronomical objects
       </a>
       are measured through their effects on local charged particles. For instance, electrons spiraling around a field line produce
       <a href="/wiki/Synchrotron_radiation" title="Synchrotron radiation">
        synchrotron radiation
       </a>
       that is detectable in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_waves" title="Radio waves">
        radio waves
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_field_lines">
        Magnetic field lines
        <span id="Magnetic_field_line">
        </span>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Magnetic field lines">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Field_line" title="Field line">
        Field line
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Magnetic_field_near_pole.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="300" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Magnetic_field_near_pole.svg/150px-Magnetic_field_near_pole.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Magnetic_field_near_pole.svg/225px-Magnetic_field_near_pole.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Magnetic_field_near_pole.svg/300px-Magnetic_field_near_pole.svg.png 2x" width="150"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Magnetic_field_near_pole.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Compass" title="Compass">
          Compasses
         </a>
         reveal the direction of the local magnetic field. As seen here, the magnetic field points towards a magnet's south pole and away from its north pole.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:854px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Magnet_bar.ogv/854px--Magnet_bar.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:854px;height:480px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="84.066666666667" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Magnet_bar.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/a/a2/Magnet_bar.ogv/Magnet_bar.ogv.480p.webm" type="video/webm;
