         <img alt="File:Magnet bar.ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Magnet_bar.ogv/220px-seek%3D35-Magnet_bar.ogv.jpg" style="width:220px;height:124px"/>
         <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a2/Magnet_bar.ogv" target="new" title="Play media">
          <span class="play-btn-large">
           <span class="mw-tmh-playtext">
            Play media
           </span>
          </span>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Magnet_bar.ogv" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Detecting magnetic field with compass and with iron filings
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-selflink selflink">
        Magnetic field
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Mapping the magnetic field of an object is simple in principle. First, measure the strength and direction of the magnetic field at a large number of locations (or at every point in space). Then, mark each location with an arrow (called a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vector_(geometry)" title="Vector (geometry)">
        vector
       </a>
       ) pointing in the direction of the local magnetic field with its magnitude proportional to the strength of the magnetic field.
      </p>
      <p>
       An alternative method to map the magnetic field is to 'connect' the arrows to form magnetic
       <i>
        field lines
       </i>
       . The direction of the magnetic field at any point is parallel to the direction of nearby field lines, and the local density of field lines can be made proportional to its strength.
      </p>
      <p>
       Magnetic field lines are like
       <a href="/wiki/Streamlines,_streaklines,_and_pathlines" title="Streamlines, streaklines, and pathlines">
        streamlines
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Fluid_dynamics" title="Fluid dynamics">
        fluid flow
       </a>
       , in that they represent something continuous, and a different resolution would show more or fewer lines. An advantage of using magnetic field lines as a representation is that many laws of magnetism (and electromagnetism) can be stated completely and concisely using simple concepts such as the 'number' of field lines through a surface. These concepts can be quickly 'translated' to their mathematical form. For example, the number of field lines through a given surface is the
       <a href="/wiki/Surface_integral" title="Surface integral">
        surface integral
       </a>
       of the magnetic field.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Magnet0873.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="298" data-file-width="444" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Magnet0873.png/220px-Magnet0873.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Magnet0873.png/330px-Magnet0873.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Magnet0873.png/440px-Magnet0873.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Magnet0873.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The direction of magnetic
         <a href="/wiki/Field_line" title="Field line">
          field lines
         </a>
         represented by the alignment of
         <a href="/wiki/Iron_filings" title="Iron filings">
          iron filings
         </a>
         sprinkled on paper placed above a bar magnet.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Various phenomena have the effect of "displaying" magnetic field lines as though the field lines were physical phenomena. For example, iron filings placed in a magnetic field, form lines that correspond to 'field lines'.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ex07_25-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ex07-25">
         [nb 6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Magnetic field "lines" are also visually displayed in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_(astronomy)" title="Aurora (astronomy)">
        polar auroras
       </a>
       , in which
       <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">
        plasma
       </a>
       particle dipole interactions create visible streaks of light that line up with the local direction of Earth's magnetic field.
      </p>
      <p>
       Field lines can be used as a qualitative tool to visualize magnetic forces. In
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferromagnetic" title="Ferromagnetic">
        ferromagnetic
       </a>
       substances like
       <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">
        iron
       </a>
       and in plasmas, magnetic forces can be understood by imagining that the field lines exert a
       <a href="/wiki/Maxwell_stress_tensor" title="Maxwell stress tensor">
        tension
       </a>
       , (like a rubber band) along their length, and a pressure perpendicular to their length on neighboring field lines. 'Unlike' poles of magnets attract because they are linked by many field lines; 'like' poles repel because their field lines do not meet, but run parallel, pushing on each other. The rigorous form of this concept is the
       <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_stress%E2%80%93energy_tensor" title="Electromagnetic stress–energy tensor">
        electromagnetic stress–energy tensor
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_field_and_permanent_magnets">
        Magnetic field and permanent magnets
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Magnetic field and permanent magnets">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Magnet" title="Magnet">
        Magnet
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       <i>
        Permanent magnets
       </i>
       are objects that produce their own persistent magnetic fields. They are made of
       <a href="/wiki/Ferromagnetism" title="Ferromagnetism">
        ferromagnetic
       </a>
       materials, such as iron and
       <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">
        nickel
       </a>
       , that have been magnetized, and they have both a north and a south pole.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_field_of_permanent_magnets">
        Magnetic field of permanent magnets
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Magnetic field of permanent magnets">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_moment" title="Magnetic moment">
        Magnetic moment
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_moment#Two_representations_of_the_cause_of_the_magnetic_moment" title="Magnetic moment">
        Two definitions of moment
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The magnetic field of permanent magnets can be quite complicated, especially near the magnet. The magnetic field of a small
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ex05_26-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ex05-26">
         [nb 7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       straight magnet is proportional to the magnet's
       <i>
        strength
       </i>
       (called its
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_dipole_moment" title="Magnetic dipole moment">
        magnetic dipole moment
       </a>
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         m
        </b>
       </span>
       ). The
       <a href="/wiki/Dipole#Field_of_a_static_magnetic_dipole" title="Dipole">
        equations
       </a>
       are non-trivial and also depend on the distance from the magnet and the orientation of the magnet. For simple magnets,
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         m
        </b>
       </span>
       points in the direction of a line drawn from the south to the north pole of the magnet. Flipping a bar magnet is equivalent to rotating its
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         m
        </b>
       </span>
       by 180 degrees.
      </p>
      <p>
       The magnetic field of larger magnets can be obtained by modelling them as a collection of a large number of small magnets called
       <a href="/wiki/Dipole" title="Dipole">
        dipoles
       </a>
       each having their own
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         m
        </b>
       </span>
       . The magnetic field produced by the magnet then is the net magnetic field of these dipoles. And, any net force on the magnet is a result of adding up the forces on the individual dipoles.
      </p>
      <p>
       There are two competing models for the nature of these dipoles. These two models produce two different magnetic fields,
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       and
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       . Outside a material, though, the two are identical (to a multiplicative constant) so that in many cases the distinction can be ignored. This is particularly true for magnetic fields, such as those due to electric currents, that are not generated by magnetic materials.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_pole_model_and_the_H-field">
        Magnetic pole model and the H-field
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Magnetic pole model and the H-field">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:VFPt_dipole_electric.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="600" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/VFPt_dipole_electric.svg/200px-VFPt_dipole_electric.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/VFPt_dipole_electric.svg/300px-VFPt_dipole_electric.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/VFPt_dipole_electric.svg/400px-VFPt_dipole_electric.svg.png 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:VFPt_dipole_electric.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The magnetic pole model: two opposing poles, North (+) and South (−), separated by a distance d produce an
         <span class="texhtml">
          <b>
           H
          </b>
         </span>
         -field (lines).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       It is sometimes useful to model the force and torques between two magnets as due to magnetic poles repelling or attracting each other in the same manner as the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coulomb_force" title="Coulomb force">
        Coulomb force
       </a>
       between electric charges. This is called the Gilbert model of magnetism, after
       <a href="/wiki/William_Gilbert_(astronomer)" title="William Gilbert (astronomer)">
        William Gilbert
       </a>
       . In this model, a magnetic
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       -field is produced by
       <i>
        magnetic charges
       </i>
       that are 'smeared' around each pole. These
       <i>
        magnetic charges
       </i>
       are in fact related to the magnetization field
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         M
        </b>
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       -field, therefore, is analogous to the
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">
        electric field
       </a>
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         E
        </b>
       </span>
       , which starts at a positive
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">
        electric charge
       </a>
       and ends at a negative electric charge. Near the north pole, therefore, all
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       -field lines point away from the north pole (whether inside the magnet or out) while near the south pole (whether inside the magnet or out) all
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       -field lines point toward the south pole. A north pole, then, feels a force in the direction of the
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       -field while the force on the south pole is opposite to the
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       -field.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the magnetic pole model, the elementary magnetic dipole
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         m
        </b>
       </span>
       is formed by two opposite magnetic poles of pole strength
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         q
        </i>
        <sub>
         m
        </sub>
       </span>
       separated by a small distance vector
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         d
        </b>
       </span>
       , such that
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         m
        </b>
        =
        <i>
         q
        </i>
        <sub>
         m
        </sub>
        <b>
         d
        </b>
       </span>
       . The magnetic pole model predicts correctly the field
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       both inside and outside magnetic materials, in particular the fact that
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       is opposite to the magnetization field
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         M
        </b>
       </span>
       inside a permanent magnet.
      </p>
      <p>
       Since it is based on the fictitious idea of a
       <i>
        magnetic charge density
       </i>
       , the Gilbert model has limitations. Magnetic poles cannot exist apart from each other as electric charges can, but always come in north/south pairs. If a magnetized object is divided in half, a new pole appears on the surface of each piece, so each has a pair of complementary poles. The magnetic pole model does not account for magnetism that is produced by electric currents.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Amperian_loop_model_and_the_B-field">
        Amperian loop model and the B-field
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Amperian loop model and the B-field">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Gauss%27s_law_for_magnetism" title="Gauss's law for magnetism">
        Gauss's law for magnetism
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:VFPt_dipole_magnetic3.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="600" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/VFPt_dipole_magnetic3.svg/200px-VFPt_dipole_magnetic3.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/VFPt_dipole_magnetic3.svg/300px-VFPt_dipole_magnetic3.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/VFPt_dipole_magnetic3.svg/400px-VFPt_dipole_magnetic3.svg.png 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:VFPt_dipole_magnetic3.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The Amperian loop model: A current loop (ring) that goes into the page at the x and comes out at the dot produces a
         <span class="texhtml">
          <b>
           B
          </b>
         </span>
         -field (lines). The north pole is to the right and the south to the left.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       After Ørsted discovered that electric currents produce a magnetic field and Ampere discovered that electric currents attracted and repelled each other similar to magnets, it was natural to hypothesize that all magnetic fields are due to electric current loops. In this model developed by Ampere, the elementary magnetic dipole that makes up all magnets is a sufficiently small Amperian loop of current I. The dipole moment of this loop is
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         m
        </i>
        =
        <i>
         IA
        </i>
       </span>
       where
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         A
        </i>
       </span>
       is the area of the loop.
      </p>
      <p>
       These magnetic dipoles produce a magnetic
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       -field. One important property of the
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       -field produced this way is that magnetic
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       -field lines neither start nor end (mathematically,
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       is a
       <a href="/wiki/Solenoidal_vector_field" title="Solenoidal vector field">
        solenoidal vector field
       </a>
       ); a field line either extends to infinity or wraps around to form a closed curve.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [nb 8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       To date, no exception to this rule has been found. (See
       <a href="#Magnetic_monopole_(hypothetical)">
        magnetic monopole
       </a>
       below.) Magnetic field lines exit a magnet near its north pole and enter near its south pole, but inside the magnet
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       -field lines continue through the magnet from the south pole back to the north.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ex08_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ex08-28">
         [nb 9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       If a
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       -field line enters a magnet somewhere it has to leave somewhere else; it is not allowed to have an end point. Magnetic poles, therefore, always come in N and S pairs.
      </p>
      <p>
       More formally, since all the magnetic field lines that enter any given region must also leave that region, subtracting the 'number'
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ex09_29-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ex09-29">
         [nb 10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       of field lines that enter the region from the number that exit gives identically zero. Mathematically this is equivalent to:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle \oint _{S}\mathbf {B} \cdot \mathrm {d} \mathbf {A} =0,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <msub>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-OP MJX-fixedlimits">
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-VCENTER">
                 <mstyle mathsize="2.07em">
                  <mtext>
                   ∮
                   <!-- ∮ -->
                  </mtext>
                  <mspace width="thinmathspace">
                  </mspace>
                 </mstyle>
                </mrow>
                <mspace width="thinmathspace">
                </mspace>
               </mrow>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 S
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo>
               ⁡
               <!-- ⁡ -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                B
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ⋅
               <!-- ⋅ -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="normal">
                d
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                A
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mn>
               0
              </mn>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle \oint _{S}\mathbf {B} \cdot \mathrm {d} \mathbf {A} =0,}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="\oint _{S}\mathbf {B} \cdot \mathrm {d} \mathbf {A} =0," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e681f61cee4c2d9fd55ce96f11a7661a07caecd8" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:17.189ex; height:6.009ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where the integral is a
       <a href="/wiki/Surface_integral" title="Surface integral">
        surface integral
       </a>
       over the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Closed_surface" title="Closed surface">
        closed surface
       </a>
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         S
        </i>
       </span>
       (a closed surface is one that completely surrounds a region with no holes to let any field lines escape). Since
       <span class="texhtml">
        d
        <b>
         A
        </b>
       </span>
       points outward, the dot product in the integral is positive for
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       -field pointing out and negative for
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       -field pointing in.
      </p>
      <p>
       There is also a corresponding differential form of this equation covered in
       <a href="#Maxwell's_equations">
        Maxwell's equations
       </a>
       below.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Force_between_magnets">
        Force between magnets
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Force between magnets">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Force_between_magnets" title="Force between magnets">
        Force between magnets
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_moment#Forces_between_two_magnetic_dipoles" title="Magnetic moment">
        force between two small magnets
       </a>
       is quite complicated and depends on the strength and orientation of both magnets and the distance and direction of the magnets relative to each other. The force is particularly sensitive to rotations of the magnets due to magnetic torque. The force on each magnet depends on its magnetic moment and the magnetic field
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ex04_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ex04-30">
         [nb 11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       of the other.
      </p>
      <p>
       To understand the force between magnets, it is useful to examine the
       <i>
        magnetic pole model
       </i>
       given above. In this model, the
       <i>
        <span class="texhtml">
         <b>
          H
         </b>
        </span>
        -field
       </i>
       of one magnet pushes and pulls on
       <i>
        both
       </i>
       poles of a second magnet. If this
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       -field is the same at both poles of the second magnet then there is no net force on that magnet since the force is opposite for opposite poles. If, however, the magnetic field of the first magnet is
       <i>
        nonuniform
       </i>
       (such as the
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       near one of its poles), each pole of the second magnet sees a different field and is subject to a different force. This difference in the two forces moves the magnet in the direction of increasing magnetic field and may also cause a net torque.
      </p>
      <p>
       This is a specific example of a general rule that magnets are attracted (or repulsed depending on the orientation of the magnet) into regions of higher magnetic field. Any non-uniform magnetic field, whether caused by permanent magnets or electric currents, exerts a force on a small magnet in this way.
      </p>
      <p>
       The details of the Amperian loop model are different and more complicated but yield the same result: that magnetic dipoles are attracted/repelled into regions of higher magnetic field. Mathematically, the force on a small magnet having a magnetic moment
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         m
        </b>
       </span>
       due to a magnetic field
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       is:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trigg_31-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Trigg-31">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {F} =\mathbf {\nabla } \left(\mathbf {m} \cdot \mathbf {B} \right),}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                F
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="normal">
                ∇
                <!-- ∇ -->
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mrow>
               <mo>
                (
               </mo>
               <mrow>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                 <mi mathvariant="bold">
                  m
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
                <mo>
                 ⋅
                 <!-- ⋅ -->
                </mo>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                 <mi mathvariant="bold">
                  B
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
               </mrow>
               <mo>
                )
               </mo>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle \mathbf {F} =\mathbf {\nabla } \left(\mathbf {m} \cdot \mathbf {B} \right),}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="\mathbf {F} =\mathbf {\nabla } \left(\mathbf {m} \cdot \mathbf {B} \right)," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b8415ca93335ab8721f9b5e1fdadeefec8f59729" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:15.754ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where the
       <a href="/wiki/Gradient" title="Gradient">
        gradient
       </a>
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         ∇
        </b>
       </span>
       is the change of the quantity
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         m
        </b>
        ·
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       per unit distance and the direction is that of maximum increase of
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         m
        </b>
        ·
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       . To understand this equation, note that the
       <a href="/wiki/Dot_product" title="Dot product">
        dot product
       </a>
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         m
        </b>
        ·
        <b>
         B
        </b>
        =
        <i>
         mB
        </i>
        cos(
        <i>
         θ
        </i>
        )
       </span>
       , where
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         m
        </i>
       </span>
       and
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         B
        </i>
       </span>
       represent the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnitude_(vector)" title="Magnitude (vector)">
        magnitude
       </a>
       of the
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         m
        </b>
       </span>
       and
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       vectors and
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         θ
        </i>
       </span>
       is the angle between them. If
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         m
        </b>
       </span>
       is in the same direction as
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       then the dot product is positive and the gradient points 'uphill' pulling the magnet into regions of higher
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       -field (more strictly larger
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         m
        </b>
        ·
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       ). This equation is strictly only valid for magnets of zero size, but is often a good approximation for not too large magnets. The magnetic force on larger magnets is determined by dividing them into smaller regions each having their own
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         m
        </b>
       </span>
       then
       <a href="/wiki/Integral" title="Integral">
        summing up the forces on each of these very small regions
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_torque_on_permanent_magnets">
        Magnetic torque on permanent magnets
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Magnetic torque on permanent magnets">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_torque" title="Magnetic torque">
        Magnetic torque
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       If two like poles of two separate magnets are brought near each other, and one of the magnets is allowed to turn, it promptly rotates to align itself with the first. In this example, the magnetic field of the stationary magnet creates a
       <i>
        magnetic torque
       </i>
       on the magnet that is free to rotate. This magnetic torque
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         τ
        </b>
       </span>
       tends to align a magnet's poles with the magnetic field lines. A compass, therefore, turns to align itself with Earth's magnetic field.
      </p>
      <p>
       Magnetic torque is used to drive
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor" title="Electric motor">
        electric motors
       </a>
       . In one simple motor design, a magnet is fixed to a freely rotating shaft and subjected to a magnetic field from an array of
       <a href="/wiki/Electromagnet" title="Electromagnet">
        electromagnets
       </a>
       . By continuously switching the electric current through each of the electromagnets, thereby flipping the polarity of their magnetic fields, like poles are kept next to the rotor; the resultant torque is transferred to the shaft. See
       <a href="#Rotating_magnetic_fields">
        Rotating magnetic fields
       </a>
       below.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dipole_in_uniform_H_field.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="210" data-file-width="465" height="99" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Dipole_in_uniform_H_field.svg/220px-Dipole_in_uniform_H_field.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Dipole_in_uniform_H_field.svg/330px-Dipole_in_uniform_H_field.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Dipole_in_uniform_H_field.svg/440px-Dipole_in_uniform_H_field.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dipole_in_uniform_H_field.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <b>
          torque on a dipole
         </b>
         : An
         <span class="texhtml">
          <b>
           H
          </b>
         </span>
         field (to right) causes equal but opposite forces on a N pole (
         <span class="texhtml">
          +
          <i>
           q
          </i>
         </span>
         ) and a S pole (
         <span class="texhtml">
          −
          <i>
           q
          </i>
         </span>
         ) creating a torque.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       As is the case for the force between magnets, the magnetic pole model leads more readily to the correct equation. Here, two equal and opposite magnetic charges experiencing the same
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       also experience equal and opposite forces. Since these equal and opposite forces are in different locations, this produces a torque proportional to the distance (perpendicular to the force) between them. With the definition of
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         m
        </b>
       </span>
       as the pole strength times the distance between the poles, this leads to
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         τ
        </i>
        =
        <i>
         μ
        </i>
        <sub>
         0
        </sub>
        <i>
         mH
        </i>
        sin
        <i>
         θ
        </i>
       </span>
       , where
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         μ
        </i>
        <sub>
         0
        </sub>
       </span>
       is a constant called the
       <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_permeability" title="Vacuum permeability">
        vacuum permeability
       </a>
       , measuring
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         6993400000000000000♠
        </span>
        4π
        <span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">
         ×
        </span>
        10
        <sup>
         −7
        </sup>
       </span>
       <a href="/wiki/Volt" title="Volt">
        V
       </a>
       ·
       <a href="/wiki/Second" title="Second">
        s
       </a>
       /(
       <a href="/wiki/Ampere" title="Ampere">
        A
       </a>
       ·
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meter" title="Meter">
        m
       </a>
       ) and
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         θ
        </i>
       </span>
       is the angle between
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       and
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         m
        </b>
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The Amperian loop model also predicts the same magnetic torque. Here, it is the
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       field interacting with the Amperian current loop through a Lorentz force described below. Again, the results are the same although the models are completely different.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cross_parallelogram.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="971" data-file-width="1110" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Cross_parallelogram.png/220px-Cross_parallelogram.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Cross_parallelogram.png/330px-Cross_parallelogram.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Cross_parallelogram.png/440px-Cross_parallelogram.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cross_parallelogram.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <b>
          Cross product
         </b>
         :
         <span class="texhtml">
          |
          <b>
           a
          </b>
          ×
          <b>
           b
          </b>
          | =
          <i>
           a b
          </i>
          sinθ
         </span>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Mathematically, the torque
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         τ
        </b>
       </span>
       on a small magnet is proportional both to the applied magnetic field and to the magnetic moment
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         m
        </b>
       </span>
       of the magnet:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle {\boldsymbol {\tau }}=\mathbf {m} \times \mathbf {B} =\mu _{0}\mathbf {m} \times \mathbf {H} ,\,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold-italic">
                τ
                <!-- τ -->
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                m
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ×
               <!-- × -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                B
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <msub>
               <mi>
                μ
                <!-- μ -->
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mn>
                 0
                </mn>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                m
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ×
               <!-- × -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                H
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
              <mspace width="thinmathspace">
              </mspace>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle {\boldsymbol {\tau }}=\mathbf {m} \times \mathbf {B} =\mu _{0}\mathbf {m} \times \mathbf {H} ,\,}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="{\boldsymbol {\tau }}=\mathbf {m} \times \mathbf {B} =\mu _{0}\mathbf {m} \times \mathbf {H} ,\," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8b2df4e74d1520e0bfd55c8f844c33c2d77c41bb" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:25.23ex; height:2.676ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where × represents the vector
       <a href="/wiki/Cross_product" title="Cross product">
        cross product
       </a>
       . Note that this equation includes all of the qualitative information included above. There is no torque on a magnet if
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         m
        </b>
       </span>
       is in the same direction as the magnetic field. (The cross product is zero for two vectors that are in the same direction.) Further, all other orientations feel a torque that twists them toward the direction of magnetic field.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_field_and_electric_currents">
        Magnetic field and electric currents
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Magnetic field and electric currents">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Currents of electric charges both generate a magnetic field and feel a force due to magnetic B-fields.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_field_due_to_moving_charges_and_electric_currents">
        Magnetic field due to moving charges and electric currents
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Magnetic field due to moving charges and electric currents">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Electromagnet" title="Electromagnet">
        Electromagnet
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Biot%E2%80%93Savart_law" title="Biot–Savart law">
        Biot–Savart law
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Amp%C3%A8re%27s_circuital_law" title="Ampère's circuital law">
        Ampère's law
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Manoderecha.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="301" data-file-width="407" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Manoderecha.svg/220px-Manoderecha.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Manoderecha.svg/330px-Manoderecha.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Manoderecha.svg/440px-Manoderecha.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Manoderecha.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right_hand_grip_rule" title="Right hand grip rule">
          Right hand grip rule
         </a>
         : a current flowing in the direction of the white arrow produces a magnetic field shown by the red arrows.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       All moving charged particles produce magnetic fields. Moving
       <a href="/wiki/Point_particle" title="Point particle">
        point
       </a>
       charges, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">
        electrons
       </a>
       , produce complicated but well known magnetic fields that depend on the charge, velocity, and acceleration of the particles.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Magnetic field lines form in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concentric" title="Concentric">
        concentric
       </a>
       circles around a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cylinder_(geometry)" title="Cylinder (geometry)">
        cylindrical
       </a>
       current-carrying conductor, such as a length of wire. The direction of such a magnetic field can be determined by using the "
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right_hand_grip_rule" title="Right hand grip rule">
        right hand grip rule
       </a>
       " (see figure at right). The strength of the magnetic field decreases with distance from the wire. (For an infinite length wire the strength is inversely proportional to the distance.)
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:52px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solenoid-1_(vertical).png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1060" data-file-width="376" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Solenoid-1_%28vertical%29.png/50px-Solenoid-1_%28vertical%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Solenoid-1_%28vertical%29.png/75px-Solenoid-1_%28vertical%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Solenoid-1_%28vertical%29.png/100px-Solenoid-1_%28vertical%29.png 2x" width="50"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solenoid-1_(vertical).png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Solenoid" title="Solenoid">
          Solenoid
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Bending a current-carrying wire into a loop concentrates the magnetic field inside the loop while weakening it outside. Bending a wire into multiple closely spaced loops to form a coil or "
       <a href="/wiki/Solenoid" title="Solenoid">
        solenoid
       </a>
       " enhances this effect. A device so formed around an iron
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_core" title="Magnetic core">
        core
       </a>
       may act as an
       <i>
        electromagnet
       </i>
       , generating a strong, well-controlled magnetic field. An infinitely long cylindrical electromagnet has a uniform magnetic field inside, and no magnetic field outside. A finite length electromagnet produces a magnetic field that looks similar to that produced by a uniform permanent magnet, with its strength and polarity determined by the current flowing through the coil.
      </p>
      <p>
       The magnetic field generated by a steady current
       <span class="texhtml">
        I
       </span>
       (a constant flow of electric charges, in which charge neither accumulates nor is depleted at any point)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ex12_33-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ex12-33">
         [nb 12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       is described by the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Biot%E2%80%93Savart_law" title="Biot–Savart law">
         Biot–Savart law
        </a>
       </i>
       :
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {B} ={\frac {\mu _{0}I}{4\pi }}\int _{\mathrm {wire} }{\frac {\mathrm {d} {\boldsymbol {\ell }}\times \mathbf {\hat {r}} }{r^{2}}},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                B
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mrow>
                 <msub>
                  <mi>
                   μ
                   <!-- μ -->
                  </mi>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mn>
                    0
                   </mn>
                  </mrow>
                 </msub>
                 <mi>
                  I
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
                <mrow>
                 <mn>
                  4
                 </mn>
                 <mi>
                  π
                  <!-- π -->
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <msub>
               <mo>
                ∫
                <!-- ∫ -->
               </mo>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                 <mi mathvariant="normal">
                  w
                 </mi>
                 <mi mathvariant="normal">
                  i
                 </mi>
                 <mi mathvariant="normal">
                  r
                 </mi>
                 <mi mathvariant="normal">
                  e
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mrow>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mi mathvariant="normal">
                   d
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mi mathvariant="bold-italic">
                   ℓ
                   <!-- ℓ -->
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                 <mo>
                  ×
                  <!-- × -->
                 </mo>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mover>
                    <mi mathvariant="bold">
                     r
                    </mi>
                    <mo mathvariant="bold" stretchy="false">
                     ^
                     <!-- ^ -->
                    </mo>
                   </mover>
                  </mrow>
                 </mrow>
                </mrow>
                <msup>
                 <mi>
                  r
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mn>
                   2
                  </mn>
                 </mrow>
                </msup>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle \mathbf {B} ={\frac {\mu _{0}I}{4\pi }}\int _{\mathrm {wire} }{\frac {\mathrm {d} {\boldsymbol {\ell }}\times \mathbf {\hat {r}} }{r^{2}}},}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="\mathbf {B} ={\frac {\mu _{0}I}{4\pi }}\int _{\mathrm {wire} }{\frac {\mathrm {d} {\boldsymbol {\ell }}\times \mathbf {\hat {r}} }{r^{2}}}," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/95838c9bca9c99ce5d48fd7282eb55b4c679abfb" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:22.835ex; height:5.843ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where the integral sums over the wire length where vector
       <span class="texhtml">
        d
        <b>
         ℓ
        </b>
       </span>
       is the vector
       <a href="/wiki/Line_element" title="Line element">
        line element
       </a>
       with direction in the same sense as the current
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         I
        </i>
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         μ
        </i>
        <sub>
         0
        </sub>
       </span>
       is the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_constant" title="Magnetic constant">
        magnetic constant
       </a>
       ,
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         r
        </i>
       </span>
       is the distance between the location of
       <span class="texhtml">
        d
        <b>
         ℓ
        </b>
       </span>
       and the location where the magnetic field is calculated, and
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         r̂
        </b>
       </span>
       is a unit vector in the direction of
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         r
        </b>
       </span>
       . In the case of a sufficiently long wire, this becomes:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {B} ={\frac {\mu _{0}I}{2\pi r}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                B
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mrow>
                 <msub>
                  <mi>
                   μ
                   <!-- μ -->
                  </mi>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mn>
                    0
                   </mn>
                  </mrow>
                 </msub>
                 <mi>
                  I
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
                <mrow>
                 <mn>
                  2
                 </mn>
                 <mi>
                  π
                  <!-- π -->
                 </mi>
                 <mi>
                  r
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle \mathbf {B} ={\frac {\mu _{0}I}{2\pi r}}}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="{\displaystyle \mathbf {B} ={\frac {\mu _{0}I}{2\pi r}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0bde76a86060392202a7ab55a2dff38c86bee9df" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:9.463ex; height:5.343ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where
       <i>
        r
       </i>
       is the distance from the wire.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSU_34-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-MSU-34">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A slightly more general
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ex13_36-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ex13-36">
         [nb 13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       way of relating the current
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle {I}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mi>
               I
              </mi>
             </mrow>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle {I}}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="{I}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/560fd148116160ac1f4e7a5a9510e2378382c81e" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.172ex; height:2.176ex;"/>
       </span>
       to the
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       -field is through
       <a href="/wiki/Amp%C3%A8re%27s_circuital_law" title="Ampère's circuital law">
        Ampère's law
       </a>
       :
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle \oint \mathbf {B} \cdot \mathrm {d} {\boldsymbol {\ell }}=\mu _{0}I_{\mathrm {enc} },}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-OP MJX-fixedlimits">
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-VCENTER">
                <mstyle mathsize="2.07em">
                 <mtext>
                  ∮
                  <!-- ∮ -->
                 </mtext>
                 <mspace width="thinmathspace">
                 </mspace>
                </mstyle>
               </mrow>
               <mspace width="thinmathspace">
               </mspace>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ⁡
               <!-- ⁡ -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                B
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ⋅
               <!-- ⋅ -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="normal">
                d
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold-italic">
                ℓ
                <!-- ℓ -->
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <msub>
               <mi>
                μ
                <!-- μ -->
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mn>
                 0
                </mn>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <msub>
               <mi>
                I
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                 <mi mathvariant="normal">
                  e
                 </mi>
                 <mi mathvariant="normal">
                  n
                 </mi>
                 <mi mathvariant="normal">
                  c
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle \oint \mathbf {B} \cdot \mathrm {d} {\boldsymbol {\ell }}=\mu _{0}I_{\mathrm {enc} },}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="\oint \mathbf {B} \cdot \mathrm {d} {\boldsymbol {\ell }}=\mu _{0}I_{\mathrm {enc} }," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/eaffba3a24a5ddbffecba6df92dadec61d58048d" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:19.902ex; height:5.843ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where the
       <a href="/wiki/Line_integral" title="Line integral">
        line integral
       </a>
       is over any arbitrary loop and
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle {I}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mi>
               I
              </mi>
             </mrow>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle {I}}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="{I}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/560fd148116160ac1f4e7a5a9510e2378382c81e" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.172ex; height:2.176ex;"/>
       </span>
       <sub>
        enc
       </sub>
       is the current enclosed by that loop. Ampère's law is always valid for steady currents and can be used to calculate the
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       -field for certain highly symmetric situations such as an infinite wire or an infinite solenoid.
      </p>
      <p>
       In a modified form that accounts for time varying electric fields, Ampère's law is one of four
       <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%27s_equations" title="Maxwell's equations">
        Maxwell's equations
       </a>
       that describe electricity and magnetism.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Force_on_moving_charges_and_current">
        Force on moving charges and current
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Force on moving charges and current">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charged-particle-drifts.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1055" data-file-width="658" height="481" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Charged-particle-drifts.svg/300px-Charged-particle-drifts.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Charged-particle-drifts.svg/450px-Charged-particle-drifts.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Charged-particle-drifts.svg/600px-Charged-particle-drifts.svg.png 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charged-particle-drifts.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <b>
          <a href="/wiki/Charge_(physics)" title="Charge (physics)">
           Charged
          </a>
          particle drifts
         </b>
         in a magnetic field with (A) no net force, (B) an electric field,
         <span class="texhtml">
          <b>
           E
          </b>
         </span>
         , (C) a charge independent force,
         <span class="texhtml">
          <b>
           F
          </b>
         </span>
         (e.g. gravity), and (D) in a homogeneous magnetic field,
         <span class="texhtml">
          grad
          <b>
           H
          </b>
         </span>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Force_on_a_charged_particle">
        Force on a charged particle
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Force on a charged particle">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Lorentz_force" title="Lorentz force">
        Lorentz force
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       A
       <a href="/wiki/Charged_particle" title="Charged particle">
        charged particle
       </a>
       moving in a
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       -field experiences a
       <i>
        sideways
       </i>
       force that is proportional to the strength of the magnetic field, the component of the velocity that is perpendicular to the magnetic field and the charge of the particle. This force is known as the
       <i>
        Lorentz force
       </i>
       , and is given by
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {F} =q\mathbf {E} +q\mathbf {v} \times \mathbf {B} ,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                F
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mi>
               q
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                E
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               +
              </mo>
              <mi>
               q
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                v
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ×
               <!-- × -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                B
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle \mathbf {F} =q\mathbf {E} +q\mathbf {v} \times \mathbf {B} ,}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="{\displaystyle \mathbf {F} =q\mathbf {E} +q\mathbf {v} \times \mathbf {B} ,}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/daba70054e9cc1b66449c534969a45695657700f" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:18.317ex; height:2.509ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         F
        </b>
       </span>
       is the
       <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">
        force
       </a>
       ,
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         q
        </i>
       </span>
       is the
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">
        electric charge
       </a>
       of the particle,
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         v
        </b>
       </span>
       is the instantaneous
       <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">
        velocity
       </a>
       of the particle, and
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       is the magnetic field (in
       <a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">
        teslas
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       The Lorentz force is always perpendicular to both the velocity of the particle and the magnetic field that created it. When a charged particle moves in a static magnetic field, it traces a helical path in which the helix axis is parallel to the magnetic field, and in which the speed of the particle remains constant. Because the magnetic force is always perpendicular to the motion, the magnetic field can do no
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mechanical_work" title="Mechanical work">
        work
       </a>
       on an isolated charge. It can only do work indirectly, via the electric field generated by a changing magnetic field. It is often claimed that the magnetic force can do work to a non-elementary
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_dipole" title="Magnetic dipole">
        magnetic dipole
       </a>
       , or to charged particles whose motion is constrained by other forces, but this is incorrect
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deissler_37-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Deissler-37">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       because the work in those cases is performed by the electric forces of the charges deflected by the magnetic field.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Force_on_current-carrying_wire">
        Force on current-carrying wire
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Force on current-carrying wire">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laplace_force" title="Laplace force">
        Laplace force
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The force on a current carrying wire is similar to that of a moving charge as expected since a charge carrying wire is a collection of moving charges. A current-carrying wire feels a force in the presence of a magnetic field. The Lorentz force on a macroscopic current is often referred to as the
       <i>
        Laplace force
       </i>
       . Consider a conductor of length
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         ℓ
        </i>
       </span>
       , cross section
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         A
        </i>
       </span>
       , and charge
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         q
        </i>
       </span>
       due to electric current
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         i
        </i>
       </span>
       . If this conductor is placed in a magnetic field of magnitude
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         B
        </i>
       </span>
       that makes an angle
       <span class="texhtml">
        θ
       </span>
       with the velocity of charges in the conductor, the force exerted on a single charge
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         q
        </i>
       </span>
       is
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle F=qvB\sin \theta ,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mi>
               F
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mi>
               q
              </mi>
              <mi>
               v
              </mi>
              <mi>
               B
              </mi>
              <mi>
               sin
              </mi>
              <mo>
               ⁡
               <!-- ⁡ -->
              </mo>
              <mi>
               θ
               <!-- θ -->
              </mi>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle F=qvB\sin \theta ,}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="F=qvB\sin \theta ," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/579e409eb2d96b820933438392094864fdc6dae2" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:14.168ex; height:2.509ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       so, for
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         N
        </i>
       </span>
       charges where
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle N=n\ell A}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mi>
               N
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mi>
               n
              </mi>
              <mi>
               ℓ
               <!-- ℓ -->
              </mi>
              <mi>
               A
              </mi>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle N=n\ell A}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="N=n\ell A" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f722917bedfa5a11ab0c1b047c698a451b0846eb" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:9.27ex; height:2.176ex;"/>
        </span>
        ,
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       the force exerted on the conductor is
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle f=FN=qvBn\ell A\sin \theta =Bi\ell \sin \theta }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mi>
               f
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>

              </mi>
              <mi>
               N
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mi>
               q
              </mi>
              <mi>
               v
              </mi>
              <mi>
               B
              </mi>
              <mi>
               n
              </mi>
              <mi>
               ℓ
               <!-- ℓ -->
              </mi>
              <mi>
               A
              </mi>
              <mi>
               sin
              </mi>
              <mo>

               <!-- ⁡ -->
              </mo>

               <!-- θ -->
              </mi>

              </mo>

              </mi>

              </mi>

               <!-- ℓ -->
              </mi>

              </mi>

            </annotation>

         <img alt="f=FN=qvBn\ell A\sin \theta =Bi\ell \sin \theta " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/eedbf2697ef6e8e23eefa31e1101291e6748b39e" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:35.424ex; height:2.509ex;"/>
