       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         i
        </i>
        =
        <i>
         nqvA
        </i>
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Regla_mano_derecha_Laplace.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="202" data-file-width="363" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Regla_mano_derecha_Laplace.svg/250px-Regla_mano_derecha_Laplace.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Regla_mano_derecha_Laplace.svg/375px-Regla_mano_derecha_Laplace.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Regla_mano_derecha_Laplace.svg/500px-Regla_mano_derecha_Laplace.svg.png 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Regla_mano_derecha_Laplace.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a href="/wiki/Right-hand_rule" title="Right-hand rule">
          right-hand rule
         </a>
         : Pointing the thumb of the right hand in the direction of the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conventional_current" title="Conventional current">
          conventional current
         </a>
         , and the fingers in the direction of
         <span class="texhtml">
          <b>
           B
          </b>
         </span>
         , the force on the current points out of the palm. The force is reversed for a negative charge.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Direction_of_force">
        Direction of force
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Direction of force">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Right-hand_rule" title="Right-hand rule">
        Right-hand rule
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The direction of force on a charge or a current can be determined by a
       <a href="/wiki/Mnemonic" title="Mnemonic">
        mnemonic
       </a>
       known as the
       <i>
        right-hand rule
       </i>
       (see the figure). Using the right hand, pointing the thumb in the direction of the current, and the fingers in the direction of the magnetic field, the resulting force on the charge points outwards from the palm. The force on a negatively charged particle is in the opposite direction. If both the speed and the charge are reversed then the direction of the force remains the same. For that reason a magnetic field measurement (by itself) cannot distinguish whether there is a positive charge moving to the right or a negative charge moving to the left. (Both of these cases produce the same current.) On the other hand, a magnetic field combined with an electric field
       <i>
        can
       </i>
       distinguish between these, see
       <a href="#Hall_effect">
        Hall effect
       </a>
       below.
      </p>
      <p>
       An alternative mnemonic to the right hand rule
       <a href="/wiki/Fleming%27s_left-hand_rule_for_motors" title="Fleming's left-hand rule for motors">
        Flemings's left hand rule
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Relation_between_H_and_B">
        Relation between H and B
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Relation between H and B">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The formulas derived for the magnetic field above are correct when dealing with the entire current. A magnetic material placed inside a magnetic field, though, generates its own
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bound_current" title="Bound current">
        bound current
       </a>
       , which can be a challenge to calculate. (This bound current is due to the sum of atomic sized current loops and the
       <a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">
        spin
       </a>
       of the subatomic particles such as electrons that make up the material.) The
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       -field as defined above helps factor out this bound current; but to see how, it helps to introduce the concept of
       <i>
        magnetization
       </i>
       first.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetization">
        Magnetization
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Magnetization">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetization" title="Magnetization">
        Magnetization
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        magnetization
       </i>
       vector field
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         M
        </b>
       </span>
       represents how strongly a region of material is magnetized. It is defined as the net
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_dipole_moment" title="Magnetic dipole moment">
        magnetic dipole moment
       </a>
       per unit volume of that region. The magnetization of a uniform magnet is therefore a material constant, equal to the magnetic moment
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         m
        </b>
       </span>
       of the magnet divided by its volume. Since the SI unit of magnetic moment is A⋅m
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       , the SI unit of magnetization
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         M
        </b>
       </span>
       is ampere per meter, identical to that of the
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       -field.
      </p>
      <p>
       The magnetization
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         M
        </b>
       </span>
       field of a region points in the direction of the average magnetic dipole moment in that region. Magnetization field lines, therefore, begin near the magnetic south pole and ends near the magnetic north pole. (Magnetization does not exist outside the magnet.)
      </p>
      <p>
       In the Amperian loop model, the magnetization is due to combining many tiny Amperian loops to form a resultant current called
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bound_current" title="Bound current">
         bound current
        </a>
       </i>
       . This bound current, then, is the source of the magnetic
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       field due to the magnet. (See
       <a href="#Magnetic_dipoles">
        Magnetic dipoles
       </a>
       below and
       <a href="/wiki/Magnet#Two_models_for_magnets:_magnetic_poles_and_atomic_currents" title="Magnet">
        magnetic poles vs. atomic currents
       </a>
       for more information.) Given the definition of the magnetic dipole, the magnetization field follows a similar law to that of Ampere's law:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle \oint \mathbf {M} \cdot \mathrm {d} {\boldsymbol {\ell }}=I_{\mathrm {b} },}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-OP MJX-fixedlimits">
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-VCENTER">
                <mstyle mathsize="2.07em">
                 <mtext>
                  ∮
                  <!-- ∮ -->
                 </mtext>
                 <mspace width="thinmathspace">
                 </mspace>
                </mstyle>
               </mrow>
               <mspace width="thinmathspace">
               </mspace>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ⁡
               <!-- ⁡ -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                M
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ⋅
               <!-- ⋅ -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="normal">
                d
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold-italic">
                ℓ
                <!-- ℓ -->
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <msub>
               <mi>
                I
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                 <mi mathvariant="normal">
                  b
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle \oint \mathbf {M} \cdot \mathrm {d} {\boldsymbol {\ell }}=I_{\mathrm {b} },}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="\oint \mathbf {M} \cdot \mathrm {d} {\boldsymbol {\ell }}=I_{\mathrm {b} }," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8617b3ca2fd2f45af47797bd11b89dbaf75f14c9" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:16.622ex; height:5.843ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where the integral is a line integral over any closed loop and
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         I
        </i>
        <sub>
         b
        </sub>
       </span>
       is the 'bound current' enclosed by that closed loop.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the magnetic pole model, magnetization begins at and ends at magnetic poles. If a given region, therefore, has a net positive 'magnetic pole strength' (corresponding to a north pole) then it has more magnetization field lines entering it than leaving it. Mathematically this is equivalent to:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle \oint _{S}\mu _{0}\mathbf {M} \cdot \mathrm {d} \mathbf {A} =-q_{\mathrm {M} }}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <msub>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-OP MJX-fixedlimits">
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-VCENTER">
                 <mstyle mathsize="2.07em">
                  <mtext>
                   ∮
                   <!-- ∮ -->
                  </mtext>
                  <mspace width="thinmathspace">
                  </mspace>
                 </mstyle>
                </mrow>
                <mspace width="thinmathspace">
                </mspace>
               </mrow>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 S
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo>
               ⁡
               <!-- ⁡ -->
              </mo>
              <msub>
               <mi>
                μ
                <!-- μ -->
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mn>
                 0
                </mn>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                M
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ⋅
               <!-- ⋅ -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="normal">
                d
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                A
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>

              </mo>

               <!-- − -->
              </mo>

               </mi>

                 <mi mathvariant="normal">
                  M
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle \oint _{S}\mu _{0}\mathbf {M} \cdot \mathrm {d} \mathbf {A} =-q_{\mathrm {M} }}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="\oint _{S}\mu _{0}\mathbf {M} \cdot \mathrm {d} \mathbf {A} =-q_{\mathrm {M} }" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9bd43f761bf6fc1c1219fd0c81f9df45de2b6159" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:23.056ex; height:6.009ex;"/>
        </span>
        ,
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where the integral is a closed surface integral over the closed surface
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         S
        </i>
       </span>
       and
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         q
        </i>
        <sub>
         M
        </sub>
       </span>
       is the 'magnetic charge' (in units of
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_flux" title="Magnetic flux">
        magnetic flux
       </a>
       ) enclosed by
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         S
        </i>
       </span>
       . (A closed surface completely surrounds a region with no holes to let any field lines escape.) The negative sign occurs because the magnetization field moves from south to north.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="H-field_and_magnetic_materials">
        H-field and magnetic materials
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">

         ]
        </span>

      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Demagnetizing_field" title="Demagnetizing field">

       where

        <b>
         H
        </b>

       does not depend at all on the bound currents.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slater_39-0">

       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">

      </p>

       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Closed_surface" title="Closed surface">
        closed surface
       </a>
       is independent of the free currents and picks out the "magnetic charges" within that closed surface:
      </p>
      <dl>

        </span>

      <p>
       which does not depend on the free currents.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       -field, therefore, can be separated into two
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [nb 14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       independent parts:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {H} =\mathbf {H} _{0}+\mathbf {H} _{\mathrm {d} },\,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                H
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <msub>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi mathvariant="bold">
                 H
                </mi>
               </mrow>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mn>
                 0
                </mn>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo>
               +
              </mo>
              <msub>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi mathvariant="bold">
                 H
                </mi>
               </mrow>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                 <mi mathvariant="normal">
                  d
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
              <mspace width="thinmathspace">
              </mspace>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle \mathbf {H} =\mathbf {H} _{0}+\mathbf {H} _{\mathrm {d} },\,}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="\mathbf{H} = \mathbf{H}_0 + \mathbf{H}_\mathrm{d}, \," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f28d629e64c21f37ca253b33c670cc4c407830cc" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:15.448ex; height:2.509ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
        <sub>
         0
        </sub>
       </span>
       is the applied magnetic field due only to the free currents and
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
        <sub>
         d
        </sub>
       </span>
       is the
       <a href="/wiki/Demagnetizing_field" title="Demagnetizing field">
        demagnetizing field
       </a>
       due only to the bound currents.
      </p>
      <p>
       The magnetic
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       -field, therefore, re-factors the bound current in terms of "magnetic charges". The
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       field lines loop only around 'free current' and, unlike the magnetic
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       field, begins and ends near magnetic poles as well.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetism">
        Magnetism
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Magnetism">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetism" title="Magnetism">
        Magnetism
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Most materials respond to an applied
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       -field by producing their own magnetization
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         M
        </b>
       </span>
       and therefore their own
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       -field. Typically, the response is weak and exists only when the magnetic field is applied. The term
       <i>
        magnetism
       </i>
       describes how materials respond on the microscopic level to an applied magnetic field and is used to categorize the magnetic
       <a href="/wiki/Phase_(matter)" title="Phase (matter)">
        phase
       </a>
       of a material. Materials are divided into groups based upon their magnetic behavior:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Diamagnetism" title="Diamagnetism">
         Diamagnetic materials
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tilley_42-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Tilley-42">
          [28]
         </a>
        </sup>
        produce a magnetization that opposes the magnetic field.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Paramagnetism" title="Paramagnetism">
         Paramagnetic materials
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tilley_42-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Tilley-42">
          [28]
         </a>
        </sup>
        produce a magnetization in the same direction as the applied magnetic field.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Ferromagnetism" title="Ferromagnetism">
         Ferromagnetic materials
        </a>
        and the closely related
        <a href="/wiki/Ferrimagnetism" title="Ferrimagnetism">
         ferrimagnetic materials
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Antiferromagnetism" title="Antiferromagnetism">
         antiferromagnetic materials
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chikazumi_43-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Chikazumi-43">
          [29]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aharoni_44-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Aharoni-44">
          [30]
         </a>
        </sup>
        can have a magnetization independent of an applied B-field with a complex relationship between the two fields.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superconductor" title="Superconductor">
         Superconductors
        </a>
        (and
        <a href="/wiki/Ferromagnetic_superconductor" title="Ferromagnetic superconductor">
         ferromagnetic superconductors
        </a>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bennemann_45-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Bennemann-45">
          [31]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewis_46-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Lewis-46">
          [32]
         </a>
        </sup>
        are materials that are characterized by perfect conductivity below a critical temperature and magnetic field. They also are highly magnetic and can be perfect diamagnets below a lower critical magnetic field. Superconductors often have a broad range of temperatures and magnetic fields (the so-named
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_II_superconductor#Mixed_state" title="Type II superconductor">
         mixed state
        </a>
        ) under which they exhibit a complex hysteretic dependence of
        <span class="texhtml">
         <b>
          M
         </b>
        </span>
        on
        <span class="texhtml">
         <b>
          B
         </b>
        </span>
        .
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       In the case of paramagnetism and diamagnetism, the magnetization
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         M
        </b>
       </span>
       is often proportional to the applied magnetic field such that:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {B} =\mu \mathbf {H} ,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                B
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mi>
               μ
               <!-- μ -->
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                H
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle \mathbf {B} =\mu \mathbf {H} ,}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="\mathbf{B} = \mu \mathbf{H}," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a575e79a4a2ae086cb8e2db0b14d3ddbd466df2d" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:9.14ex; height:2.676ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         μ
        </i>
       </span>
       is a material dependent parameter called the
       <a href="/wiki/Permeability_(electromagnetism)" title="Permeability (electromagnetism)">
        permeability
       </a>
       . In some cases the permeability may be a second rank
       <a href="/wiki/Tensor" title="Tensor">
        tensor
       </a>
       so that
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       may not point in the same direction as
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       . These relations between
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       and
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       are examples of
       <a href="/wiki/Constitutive_equation" title="Constitutive equation">
        constitutive equations
       </a>
       . However, superconductors and ferromagnets have a more complex
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       to
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       relation; see
       <a href="/wiki/Hysteresis#Magnetic_hysteresis" title="Hysteresis">
        magnetic hysteresis
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_stored_in_magnetic_fields">
        Energy stored in magnetic fields
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Energy stored in magnetic fields">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_energy" title="Magnetic energy">
        Magnetic energy
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Hysteresis#Magnetic_hysteresis" title="Hysteresis">
        Magnetic hysteresis
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Energy is needed to generate a magnetic field both to work against the electric field that a changing magnetic field creates and to change the magnetization of any material within the magnetic field. For non-dispersive materials, this same energy is released when the magnetic field is destroyed so that this energy can be modeled as being stored in the magnetic field.
      </p>
      <p>
       For linear, non-dispersive, materials (such that
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
        =
        <i>
         μ
        </i>
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       where
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         μ
        </i>
       </span>
       is frequency-independent), the
       <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">
        energy density
       </a>
       is:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle u={\frac {\mathbf {B} \cdot \mathbf {H} }{2}}={\frac {\mathbf {B} \cdot \mathbf {B} }{2\mu }}={\frac {\mu \mathbf {H} \cdot \mathbf {H} }{2}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mi>
               u
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mrow>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mi mathvariant="bold">
                   B
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                 <mo>
                  ⋅
                  <!-- ⋅ -->
                 </mo>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mi mathvariant="bold">
                   H
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                </mrow>
                <mn>
                 2
                </mn>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mrow>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mi mathvariant="bold">
                   B
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                 <mo>
                  ⋅
                  <!-- ⋅ -->
                 </mo>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mi mathvariant="bold">
                   B
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                </mrow>
                <mrow>
                 <mn>
                  2
                 </mn>
                 <mi>
                  μ
                  <!-- μ -->
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mrow>
                 <mi>
                  μ
                  <!-- μ -->
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mi mathvariant="bold">
                   H
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                 <mo>
                  ⋅
                  <!-- ⋅ -->
                 </mo>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mi mathvariant="bold">
                   H
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                </mrow>
                <mn>
                 2
                </mn>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               .
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle u={\frac {\mathbf {B} \cdot \mathbf {H} }{2}}={\frac {\mathbf {B} \cdot \mathbf {B} }{2\mu }}={\frac {\mu \mathbf {H} \cdot \mathbf {H} }{2}}.}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt=" u = \frac{\mathbf{B}\cdot\mathbf{H}}{2}= \frac{\mathbf{B}\cdot\mathbf{B}}{2\mu} = \frac{\mu\mathbf{H}\cdot\mathbf{H}}{2}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/56d2a975eb0ed09b79a50affd4cfe3deb0f238fd" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:32.197ex; height:5.843ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       If there are no magnetic materials around then
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         μ
        </i>
       </span>
       can be replaced by
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         μ
        </i>
        <sub>
         0
        </sub>
       </span>
       . The above equation cannot be used for nonlinear materials, though; a more general expression given below must be used.
      </p>
      <p>
       In general, the incremental amount of work per unit volume
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         δW
        </i>
       </span>
       needed to cause a small change of magnetic field
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         δ
        </i>
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       is:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle \delta W=\mathbf {H} \cdot \delta \mathbf {B} .}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mi>
               δ
               <!-- δ -->
              </mi>
              <mi>
               W
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                H
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ⋅
               <!-- ⋅ -->
              </mo>
              <mi>
               δ
               <!-- δ -->
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                B
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               .
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle \delta W=\mathbf {H} \cdot \delta \mathbf {B} .}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="\delta W=\mathbf {H} \cdot \delta \mathbf {B} ." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/38cbbaed7511fa5fcbe6fd25a9c11a643ca16027" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:13.949ex; height:2.343ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Once the relationship between
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       and
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       is known this equation is used to determine the work needed to reach a given magnetic state. For
       <a href="/wiki/Hysteresis" title="Hysteresis">
        hysteretic materials
       </a>
       such as ferromagnets and superconductors, the work needed also depends on how the magnetic field is created. For linear non-dispersive materials, though, the general equation leads directly to the simpler energy density equation given above.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Electromagnetism:_the_relationship_between_magnetic_and_electric_fields">
        Electromagnetism: the relationship between magnetic and electric fields
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Electromagnetism: the relationship between magnetic and electric fields">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">
        Electromagnetism
       </a>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span id="Faraday.27s_Law:_Electric_force_due_to_a_changing_B-field">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Faraday's_Law:_Electric_force_due_to_a_changing_B-field">
        Faraday's Law: Electric force due to a changing B-field
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Faraday's Law: Electric force due to a changing B-field">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Faraday%27s_law_of_induction" title="Faraday's law of induction">
        Faraday's law
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_flux" title="Magnetic flux">
        Magnetic flux
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       A changing magnetic field, such as a magnet moving through a conducting coil, generates an
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">
        electric field
       </a>
       (and therefore tends to drive a current in such a coil). This is known as
       <i>
        Faraday's law
       </i>
       and forms the basis of many
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_generator" title="Electrical generator">
        electrical generators
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor" title="Electric motor">
        electric motors
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Mathematically, Faraday's law is:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle {\mathcal {E}}=-{\frac {\mathrm {d} \Phi _{\mathrm {m} }}{\mathrm {d} t}},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi class="MJX-tex-caligraphic" mathvariant="script">
                 E
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mo>
               −
               <!-- − -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mrow>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mi mathvariant="normal">
                   d
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                 <msub>
                  <mi mathvariant="normal">
                   Φ
                   <!-- Φ -->
                  </mi>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                    <mi mathvariant="normal">
                     m
                    </mi>
                   </mrow>
                  </mrow>
                 </msub>
                </mrow>
                <mrow>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mi mathvariant="normal">
                   d
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                 <mi>
                  t
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle {\mathcal {E}}=-{\frac {\mathrm {d} \Phi _{\mathrm {m} }}{\mathrm {d} t}},}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="\mathcal{E} = - \frac{\mathrm{d}\Phi_\mathrm{m}}{\mathrm{d}t}," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3b92d58a3a908fe0c179221888f961a67e138ee6" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:12.272ex; height:5.509ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle \scriptstyle {\mathcal {E}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="1">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi class="MJX-tex-caligraphic" mathvariant="script">
                 E
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </mrow>
             </mstyle>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle \scriptstyle {\mathcal {E}}}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="\scriptstyle\mathcal{E}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0e21b4522cc0f5cb6756ced273b078c1e4c7f239" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:0.927ex; height:1.676ex;"/>
       </span>
       is the
       <a href="/wiki/Electromotive_force" title="Electromotive force">
        electromotive force
       </a>
       (or
       <i>
        EMF
       </i>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Voltage" title="Voltage">
        voltage
       </a>
       generated around a closed loop) and
       <span class="texhtml">
        Φ
        <sub>
         m
        </sub>
       </span>
       is the
       <i>
        magnetic flux
       </i>
       —the product of the area times the magnetic field
       <a href="/wiki/Tangential_and_normal_components" title="Tangential and normal components">
        normal
       </a>
       to that area. (This definition of magnetic flux is why
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       is often referred to as
       <i>
        magnetic flux density
       </i>
       .)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         210
        </span>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The negative sign represents the fact that any current generated by a changing magnetic field in a coil produces a magnetic field that
       <i>
        opposes
       </i>
       the
       <i>
        change
       </i>
       in the magnetic field that induced it. This phenomenon is known as
       <a href="/wiki/Lenz%27s_law" title="Lenz's law">
        Lenz's law
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       This integral formulation of Faraday's law can be converted
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ex14_48-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ex14-48">
         [nb 15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       into a differential form, which applies under slightly different conditions. This form is covered as one of Maxwell's equations below.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Maxwell.27s_correction_to_Amp.C3.A8re.27s_Law:_The_magnetic_field_due_to_a_changing_electric_field">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Maxwell's_correction_to_Ampère's_Law:_The_magnetic_field_due_to_a_changing_electric_field">
        Maxwell's correction to Ampère's Law: The magnetic field due to a changing electric field
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Maxwell's correction to Ampère's Law: The magnetic field due to a changing electric field">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Amp%C3%A8re%27s_circuital_law#Extending_the_original_law:_the_Maxwell–Ampère_equation" title="Ampère's circuital law">
        Maxwell's correction to Ampère's law
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Similar to the way that a changing magnetic field generates an electric field, a changing electric field generates a magnetic field. This fact is known as
       <i>
        Maxwell's correction to Ampère's law
       </i>
       and is applied as an additive term to Ampere's law as given above. This additional term is proportional to the time rate of change of the electric flux and is similar to Faraday's law above but with a different and positive constant out front. (The electric flux through an area is proportional to the area times the perpendicular part of the electric field.)
      </p>
      <p>
       The full law including the correction term is known as the Maxwell–Ampère equation. It is not commonly given in integral form because the effect is so small that it can typically be ignored in most cases where the integral form is used.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Maxwell term
       <i>
        is
       </i>
       critically important in the creation and propagation of electromagnetic waves. Maxwell's correction to Ampère's Law together with Faraday's law of induction describes how mutually changing electric and magnetic fields interact to sustain each other and thus to form
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_waves" title="Electromagnetic waves">
        electromagnetic waves
       </a>
       , such as light: a changing electric field generates a changing magnetic field, which generates a changing electric field again. These, though, are usually described using the differential form of this equation given below.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Maxwell.27s_equations">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Maxwell's_equations">
        Maxwell's equations
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Maxwell's equations">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%27s_equations" title="Maxwell's equations">
        Maxwell's equations
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Like all vector fields, a magnetic field has two important mathematical properties that relates it to its
       <i>
        sources
       </i>
       . (For
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       the
       <i>
        sources
       </i>
       are currents and changing electric fields.) These two properties, along with the two corresponding properties of the electric field, make up
       <i>
        Maxwell's Equations
       </i>
       . Maxwell's Equations together with the Lorentz force law form a complete description of
       <a href="/wiki/Classical_electromagnetism" title="Classical electromagnetism">
        classical electrodynamics
       </a>
       including both electricity and magnetism.
      </p>
      <p>
       The first property is the
       <a href="/wiki/Divergence" title="Divergence">
        divergence
       </a>
       of a vector field
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         A
        </b>
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         ∇
        </b>
        ·
        <b>
         A
        </b>
       </span>
       , which represents how
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         A
        </b>
       </span>
       'flows' outward from a given point. As discussed above, a
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       -field line never starts or ends at a point but instead forms a complete loop. This is mathematically equivalent to saying that the divergence of
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       is zero. (Such vector fields are called
       <a href="/wiki/Solenoidal_vector_field" title="Solenoidal vector field">
        solenoidal vector fields
       </a>
       .) This property is called
       <a href="/wiki/Gauss%27s_law_for_magnetism" title="Gauss's law for magnetism">
        Gauss's law for magnetism
       </a>
       and is equivalent to the statement that there are no isolated magnetic poles or
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_monopole" title="Magnetic monopole">
        magnetic monopoles
       </a>
       . The electric field on the other hand begins and ends at electric charges so that its divergence is non-zero and proportional to the
       <a href="/wiki/Charge_density" title="Charge density">
        charge density
       </a>
       (See
       <a href="/wiki/Gauss%27s_law" title="Gauss's law">
        Gauss's law
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       The second mathematical property is called the
       <a href="/wiki/Curl_(mathematics)" title="Curl (mathematics)">
        curl
       </a>
       , such that
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         ∇
        </b>
        ×
        <b>
         A
        </b>
       </span>
       represents how
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         A
        </b>
       </span>
       curls or 'circulates' around a given point. The result of the curl is called a 'circulation source'. The equations for the curl of
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       and of
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         E
        </b>
       </span>
       are called the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amp%C3%A8re%E2%80%93Maxwell_equation" title="Ampère–Maxwell equation">
        Ampère–Maxwell equation
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Faraday%27s_law_of_induction" title="Faraday's law of induction">
        Faraday's law
       </a>
       respectively. They represent the differential forms of the integral equations given above.
      </p>
      <p>
       The complete set of Maxwell's equations then are:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">

                 </mtd>
                </mtr>
                <mtr>
                 <mtd>
                  <mi mathvariant="normal">

                   <!-- × -->
                  </mo>

                   </mi>
                  </mrow>

                 <mtd>

                  </mo>

                   <!-- − -->
                  </mo>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

      <p>
       where
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         J
        </b>
       </span>
       = complete microscopic
       <a href="/wiki/Current_density" title="Current density">
        current density
       </a>
       and
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         ρ
        </i>
       </span>
       is the charge density.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BIsAPseudovector.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="520" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/BIsAPseudovector.svg/220px-BIsAPseudovector.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/BIsAPseudovector.svg/330px-BIsAPseudovector.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/BIsAPseudovector.svg/440px-BIsAPseudovector.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BIsAPseudovector.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <b>
          Magnetic field, like all
          <a href="/wiki/Pseudovector" title="Pseudovector">
           pseudovectors
          </a>
          , changes sign when reflected in a mirror
         </b>
         : When a
         <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">
          current
         </a>
         carrying loop (black) is
         <a href="/wiki/Mirror_image" title="Mirror image">
          reflected in a mirror
         </a>
         (dotted line), its magnetic field (blue) is reflected
         <i>
          and
         </i>
         reversed.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Technically,
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       is a
       <a href="/wiki/Pseudovector" title="Pseudovector">
        pseudovector
       </a>
       (also called an
       <i>
        axial vector
       </i>
       ) due to being defined by a vector cross product. (See diagram.)
      </p>
      <p>
       As discussed above, materials respond to an applied electric
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         E
        </b>
       </span>
       field and an applied magnetic
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       field by producing their own internal 'bound' charge and current distributions that contribute to
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         E
        </b>
       </span>
       and
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       but are difficult to calculate. To circumvent this problem,
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       and
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         D
        </b>
       </span>
       fields are used to re-factor Maxwell's equations in terms of the
       <i>
        free current density
       </i>
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         J
        </b>
        <sub>
         f
        </sub>
       </span>
       and
       <i>
        free charge density
       </i>
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         ρ
        </i>
        <sub>
         f
        </sub>
       </span>
       :
      </p>
