          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="{\displaystyle {\begin{aligned}\nabla \cdot \mathbf {B} &amp;=0,\\\nabla \cdot \mathbf {D} &amp;=\rho _{\mathrm {f} },\\\nabla \times \mathbf {H} &amp;=\mathbf {J} _{\mathrm {f} }+{\frac {\partial \mathbf {D} }{\partial t}},\\\nabla \times \mathbf {E} &amp;=-{\frac {\partial \mathbf {B} }{\partial t}}.\end{aligned}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bc30a872eb957fbc7ee86cff6b76deb62d3014f0" style="vertical-align: -8.005ex; width:20.634ex; height:17.176ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       These equations are not any more general than the original equations (if the 'bound' charges and currents in the material are known). They also must be supplemented by the relationship between
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       and
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       as well as that between
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         E
        </b>
       </span>
       and
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         D
        </b>
       </span>
       . On the other hand, for simple relationships between these quantities this form of Maxwell's equations can circumvent the need to calculate the bound charges and currents.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Electric_and_magnetic_fields:_different_aspects_of_the_same_phenomenon">
        Electric and magnetic fields: different aspects of the same phenomenon
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Electric and magnetic fields: different aspects of the same phenomenon">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Relativistic_electromagnetism" title="Relativistic electromagnetism">
        Relativistic electromagnetism
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       According to
       <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">
        the special theory of relativity
       </a>
       , the partition of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_force" title="Electromagnetic force">
        electromagnetic force
       </a>
       into separate electric and magnetic components is not fundamental, but varies with the
       <a href="/wiki/Frame_of_reference#Observational_frames_of_reference" title="Frame of reference">
        observational frame of reference
       </a>
       : An electric force perceived by one observer may be perceived by another (in a different frame of reference) as a magnetic force, or a mixture of electric and magnetic forces.
      </p>
      <p>
       Formally, special relativity combines the electric and magnetic fields into a rank-2
       <a href="/wiki/Tensor" title="Tensor">
        tensor
       </a>
       , called the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_tensor" title="Electromagnetic tensor">
         electromagnetic tensor
        </a>
       </i>
       . Changing reference frames
       <i>
        mixes
       </i>
       these components. This is analogous to the way that special relativity
       <i>
        mixes
       </i>
       space and time into
       <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">
        spacetime
       </a>
       , and mass, momentum and energy into
       <a href="/wiki/Four-momentum" title="Four-momentum">
        four-momentum
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_vector_potential">
        Magnetic vector potential
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Magnetic vector potential">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_potential" title="Magnetic potential">
        Magnetic potential
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In advanced topics such as
       <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">
        quantum mechanics
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_relativity" title="Theory of relativity">
        relativity
       </a>
       it is often easier to work with a potential formulation of electrodynamics rather than in terms of the electric and magnetic fields. In this representation, the
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_vector_potential" title="Magnetic vector potential">
         vector potential
        </a>
       </i>
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         A
        </b>
       </span>
       , and the
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_potential" title="Electric potential">
        scalar potential
       </a>
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         φ
        </i>
       </span>
       , are defined such that:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle {\begin{aligned}\mathbf {B} &amp;=\nabla \times \mathbf {A} ,\\\mathbf {E} &amp;=-\nabla \varphi -{\frac {\partial \mathbf {A} }{\partial t}}.\end{aligned}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mtable columnalign="right left right left right left right left right left right left" columnspacing="0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em" displaystyle="true" rowspacing="3pt">
                <mtr>
                 <mtd>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mi mathvariant="bold">
                    B
                   </mi>
                  </mrow>
                 </mtd>
                 <mtd>
                  <mi>
                  </mi>
                  <mo>
                   =
                  </mo>
                  <mi mathvariant="normal">
                   ∇
                   <!-- ∇ -->
                  </mi>
                  <mo>
                   ×
                   <!-- × -->
                  </mo>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mi mathvariant="bold">
                    A
                   </mi>
                  </mrow>
                  <mo>
                   ,
                  </mo>
                 </mtd>
                </mtr>
                <mtr>
                 <mtd>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mi mathvariant="bold">
                    E
                   </mi>
                  </mrow>
                 </mtd>
                 <mtd>
                  <mi>
                  </mi>
                  <mo>
                   =
                  </mo>
                  <mo>
                   −
                   <!-- − -->
                  </mo>
                  <mi mathvariant="normal">
                   ∇
                   <!-- ∇ -->
                  </mi>
                  <mi>
                   φ
                   <!-- φ -->
                  </mi>
                  <mo>
                   −
                   <!-- − -->
                  </mo>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mfrac>
                    <mrow>
                     <mi mathvariant="normal">
                      ∂
                      <!-- ∂ -->
                     </mi>
                     <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                      <mi mathvariant="bold">
                       A
                      </mi>
                     </mrow>
                    </mrow>
                    <mrow>
                     <mi mathvariant="normal">
                      ∂
                      <!-- ∂ -->
                     </mi>
                     <mi>
                      t
                     </mi>
                    </mrow>
                   </mfrac>
                  </mrow>
                  <mo>
                   .
                  </mo>
                 </mtd>
                </mtr>
               </mtable>
              </mrow>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle {\begin{aligned}\mathbf {B} &amp;=\nabla \times \mathbf {A} ,\\\mathbf {E} &amp;=-\nabla \varphi -{\frac {\partial \mathbf {A} }{\partial t}}.\end{aligned}}}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="{\displaystyle {\begin{aligned}\mathbf {B} &amp;=\nabla \times \mathbf {A} ,\\\mathbf {E} &amp;=-\nabla \varphi -{\frac {\partial \mathbf {A} }{\partial t}}.\end{aligned}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/727409f6151fc24d6f89c41cb664b4538cc45771" style="vertical-align: -3.427ex; margin-bottom: -0.244ex; width:18.676ex; height:8.509ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       The vector potential
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         A
        </b>
       </span>
       may be interpreted as a
       <i>
        generalized potential
        <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">
         momentum
        </a>
        per unit charge
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       just as
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         φ
        </i>
       </span>
       is interpreted as a
       <i>
        generalized
        <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy" title="Potential energy">
         potential energy
        </a>
        per unit charge
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Maxwell's equations when expressed in terms of the potentials can be cast into a form that agrees with
       <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">
        special relativity
       </a>
       with little effort.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In relativity
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         A
        </b>
       </span>
       together with
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         φ
        </i>
       </span>
       forms the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Four-potential" title="Four-potential">
        four-potential
       </a>
       , analogous to the
       <a href="/wiki/Four-vector#Four-momentum" title="Four-vector">
        four-momentum
       </a>
       that combines the momentum and energy of a particle. Using the four potential instead of the electromagnetic tensor has the advantage of being much simpler—and it can be easily modified to work with quantum mechanics.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Quantum_electrodynamics">
        Quantum electrodynamics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Quantum electrodynamics">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">
        Standard Model
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Quantum_electrodynamics" title="Quantum electrodynamics">
        quantum electrodynamics
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In modern physics, the electromagnetic field is understood to be not a
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Classical_physics" title="Classical physics">
         classical
        </a>
       </i>
       <a href="/wiki/Field_(physics)" title="Field (physics)">
        field
       </a>
       , but rather a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantum_field" title="Quantum field">
        quantum field
       </a>
       ; it is represented not as a vector of three
       <a href="/wiki/Real_number" title="Real number">
        numbers
       </a>
       at each point, but as a vector of three
       <a href="/wiki/Operator_(physics)" title="Operator (physics)">
        quantum operators
       </a>
       at each point. The most accurate modern description of the electromagnetic interaction (and much else) is
       <i>
        quantum electrodynamics
       </i>
       (QED),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which is incorporated into a more complete theory known as the
       <i>
        Standard Model of particle physics
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In QED, the magnitude of the electromagnetic interactions between charged particles (and their
       <a href="/wiki/Antiparticle" title="Antiparticle">
        antiparticles
       </a>
       ) is computed using
       <a href="/wiki/Perturbation_theory_(quantum_mechanics)" title="Perturbation theory (quantum mechanics)">
        perturbation theory
       </a>
       . These rather complex formulas produce a remarkable pictorial representation as
       <a href="/wiki/Feynman_diagram" title="Feynman diagram">
        Feynman diagrams
       </a>
       in which
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virtual_photon" title="Virtual photon">
        virtual photons
       </a>
       are exchanged.
      </p>
      <p>
       Predictions of QED agree with experiments to an extremely high degree of accuracy: currently about 10
       <sup>
        −12
       </sup>
       (and limited by experimental errors); for details see
       <a href="/wiki/Precision_tests_of_QED" title="Precision tests of QED">
        precision tests of QED
       </a>
       . This makes QED one of the most accurate physical theories constructed thus far.
      </p>
      <p>
       All equations in this article are in the
       <a href="/wiki/Classical_electromagnetism" title="Classical electromagnetism">
        classical approximation
       </a>
       , which is less accurate than the quantum description mentioned here. However, under most everyday circumstances, the difference between the two theories is negligible.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Important_uses_and_examples_of_magnetic_field">
        Important uses and examples of magnetic field
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Important uses and examples of magnetic field">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span id="Earth.27s_magnetic_field">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Earth's_magnetic_field">
        Earth's magnetic field
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Earth's magnetic field">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth's magnetic field">
        Earth's magnetic field
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Earths_Magnetic_Field_Confusion.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="320" height="291" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Earths_Magnetic_Field_Confusion.svg/310px-Earths_Magnetic_Field_Confusion.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Earths_Magnetic_Field_Confusion.svg/465px-Earths_Magnetic_Field_Confusion.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Earths_Magnetic_Field_Confusion.svg/620px-Earths_Magnetic_Field_Confusion.svg.png 2x" width="310"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Earths_Magnetic_Field_Confusion.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A sketch of Earth's magnetic field representing the source of the field as a magnet. The geographic north pole of Earth is near the top of the diagram, the south pole near the bottom. The south pole of that magnet is deep in Earth's interior below Earth's North Magnetic Pole.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Earth's magnetic field is thought to be produced by convection currents in the outer liquid of Earth's core. The
       <a href="/wiki/Dynamo_theory" title="Dynamo theory">
        Dynamo theory
       </a>
       proposes that these movements produce electric currents that, in turn, produce the magnetic field.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The presence of this field causes a compass, placed anywhere within it, to rotate so that the "north pole" of the magnet in the compass points roughly north, toward Earth's
       <a href="/wiki/North_Magnetic_Pole" title="North Magnetic Pole">
        North Magnetic Pole
       </a>
       . This is the traditional definition of the "north pole" of a magnet, although other equivalent definitions are also possible.
      </p>
      <p>
       One confusion that arises from this definition is that, if Earth itself is considered as a magnet, the
       <i>
        south
       </i>
       pole of that magnet would be the one nearer the north magnetic pole, and vice versa. The north magnetic pole is so-named not because of the polarity of the field there but because of its geographical location. The north and south poles of a permanent magnet are so-called because they are "north-seeking" and "south-seeking", respectively.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The figure is a sketch of Earth's magnetic field represented by field lines. For most locations, the magnetic field has a significant up/down component in addition to the north/south component. (There is also an east/west component, as Earth's magnetic and geographical poles do not coincide.) The magnetic field can be visualised as a bar magnet buried deep in Earth's interior.
      </p>
      <p>
       Earth's magnetic field is not constant—the strength of the field and the location of its poles vary. Moreover, the poles periodically reverse their orientation in a process called
       <a href="/wiki/Geomagnetic_reversal" title="Geomagnetic reversal">
        geomagnetic reversal
       </a>
       . The
       <a href="/wiki/Brunhes%E2%80%93Matuyama_reversal" title="Brunhes–Matuyama reversal">
        most recent reversal
       </a>
       occurred 780,000 years ago.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Rotating_magnetic_fields">
        Rotating magnetic fields
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Rotating magnetic fields">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Rotating_magnetic_field" title="Rotating magnetic field">
        Rotating magnetic field
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Alternator" title="Alternator">
        Alternator
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        rotating magnetic field
       </i>
       is a key principle in the operation of
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor#AC_motors" title="Electric motor">
        alternating-current motors
       </a>
       . A permanent magnet in such a field rotates so as to maintain its alignment with the external field. This effect was conceptualized by
       <a href="/wiki/Nikola_Tesla" title="Nikola Tesla">
        Nikola Tesla
       </a>
       , and later utilized in his, and others', early AC (
       <a href="/wiki/Alternating_current" title="Alternating current">
        alternating current
       </a>
       ) electric motors.
      </p>
      <p>
       A rotating magnetic field can be constructed using two orthogonal coils with 90 degrees phase difference in their AC currents. However, in practice such a system would be supplied through a three-wire arrangement with unequal currents.
      </p>
      <p>
       This inequality would cause serious problems in standardization of the conductor size and so, to overcome it,
       <a href="/wiki/Three-phase" title="Three-phase">
        three-phase
       </a>
       systems are used where the three currents are equal in magnitude and have 120 degrees phase difference. Three similar coils having mutual geometrical angles of 120 degrees create the rotating magnetic field in this case. The ability of the three-phase system to create a rotating field, utilized in electric motors, is one of the main reasons why three-phase systems dominate the world's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_power" title="Electrical power">
        electrical power
       </a>
       supply systems.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Synchronous_motor" title="Synchronous motor">
        Synchronous motors
       </a>
       use DC-voltage-fed rotor windings, which lets the excitation of the machine be controlled—and
       <a href="/wiki/Induction_motor" title="Induction motor">
        induction motors
       </a>
       use short-circuited
       <a href="/wiki/Rotor_(electric)" title="Rotor (electric)">
        rotors
       </a>
       (instead of a magnet) following the rotating magnetic field of a multicoiled
       <a href="/wiki/Stator" title="Stator">
        stator
       </a>
       . The short-circuited turns of the rotor develop
       <a href="/wiki/Eddy_current" title="Eddy current">
        eddy currents
       </a>
       in the rotating field of the stator, and these currents in turn move the rotor by the Lorentz force.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1882, Nikola Tesla identified the concept of the rotating magnetic field. In 1885,
       <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Ferraris" title="Galileo Ferraris">
        Galileo Ferraris
       </a>
       independently researched the concept. In 1888, Tesla gained
       <span>
        <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/patents/US381968" rel="nofollow">
         U.S. Patent 381,968
        </a>
       </span>
       for his work. Also in 1888, Ferraris published his research in a paper to the
       <i>
        Royal Academy of Sciences
       </i>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Turin" title="Turin">
        Turin
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Hall_effect">
        Hall effect
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Hall effect">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Hall_effect" title="Hall effect">
        Hall effect
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The charge carriers of a current-carrying conductor placed in a transverse magnetic field experience a sideways Lorentz force; this results in a charge separation in a direction perpendicular to the current and to the magnetic field. The resultant voltage in that direction is proportional to the applied magnetic field. This is known as the
       <i>
        Hall effect
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        Hall effect
       </i>
       is often used to measure the magnitude of a magnetic field. It is used as well to find the sign of the dominant charge carriers in materials such as semiconductors (negative electrons or positive holes).
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_circuits">
        Magnetic circuits
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Magnetic circuits">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_circuit" title="Magnetic circuit">
        Magnetic circuit
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       An important use of
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       is in
       <i>
        magnetic circuits
       </i>
       where
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
        =
        <i>
         μ
        </i>
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       inside a linear material. Here,
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         μ
        </i>
       </span>
       is the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_permeability" title="Magnetic permeability">
        magnetic permeability
       </a>
       of the material. This result is similar in form to
       <a href="/wiki/Ohm%27s_law" title="Ohm's law">
        Ohm's law
       </a>
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         J
        </b>
        =
        <i>
         σ
        </i>
        <b>
         E
        </b>
       </span>
       , where
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         J
        </b>
       </span>
       is the current density,
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         σ
        </i>
       </span>
       is the conductance and
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         E
        </b>
       </span>
       is the electric field. Extending this analogy, the counterpart to the macroscopic Ohm's law (
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         I
        </i>
        =
        <i>
         V
        </i>
        ⁄
        <i>
         R
        </i>
       </span>
       ) is:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle \Phi ={\frac {F}{R}}_{\mathrm {m} },}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mi mathvariant="normal">
               Φ
               <!-- Φ -->
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <msub>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mfrac>
                 <mi>
                  F
                 </mi>
                 <mi>
                  R
                 </mi>
                </mfrac>
               </mrow>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                 <mi mathvariant="normal">
                  m
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle \Phi ={\frac {F}{R}}_{\mathrm {m} },}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="\Phi = \frac F R_\mathrm{m}," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/18a0f4b5e7c42f2f7ba26fca357bac5ed9a2cf6a" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:9.625ex; height:5.343ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle \Phi =\int \mathbf {B} \cdot \mathrm {d} \mathbf {A} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi mathvariant="normal">
              Φ
              <!-- Φ -->
             </mi>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <mo>
              ∫
              <!-- ∫ -->
             </mo>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mi mathvariant="bold">
               B
              </mi>
             </mrow>
             <mo>
              ⋅
              <!-- ⋅ -->
             </mo>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mi mathvariant="normal">
               d
              </mi>
             </mrow>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mi mathvariant="bold">
               A
              </mi>
             </mrow>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle \Phi =\int \mathbf {B} \cdot \mathrm {d} \mathbf {A} }
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="\Phi = \int \mathbf{B}\cdot \mathrm{d}\mathbf{A}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/51615565ad481a7bd6db7a202e648ccfcd54b2be" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:14.249ex; height:5.676ex;"/>
       </span>
       is the magnetic flux in the circuit,
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle F=\int \mathbf {H} \cdot \mathrm {d} {\boldsymbol {\ell }}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              F
             </mi>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <mo>
              ∫
              <!-- ∫ -->
             </mo>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mi mathvariant="bold">
               H
              </mi>
             </mrow>
             <mo>
              ⋅
              <!-- ⋅ -->
             </mo>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mi mathvariant="normal">
               d
              </mi>
             </mrow>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mi mathvariant="bold-italic">
               ℓ
               <!-- ℓ -->
              </mi>
             </mrow>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle F=\int \mathbf {H} \cdot \mathrm {d} {\boldsymbol {\ell }}}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="F = \int \mathbf{H}\cdot \mathrm{d}\boldsymbol{\ell}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81f090edfe892fff2c3a70a1f78b12f9b57434fd" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:13.585ex; height:5.676ex;"/>
       </span>
       is the
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetomotive_force" title="Magnetomotive force">
        magnetomotive force
       </a>
       applied to the circuit, and
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         R
        </i>
        <sub>
         m
        </sub>
       </span>
       is the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reluctance" title="Reluctance">
        reluctance
       </a>
       of the circuit. Here the reluctance
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         R
        </i>
        <sub>
         m
        </sub>
       </span>
       is a quantity similar in nature to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_resistance" title="Electrical resistance">
        resistance
       </a>
       for the flux.
      </p>
      <p>
       Using this analogy it is straightforward to calculate the magnetic flux of complicated magnetic field geometries, by using all the available techniques of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circuit_theory" title="Circuit theory">
        circuit theory
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_field_shape_descriptions">
        Magnetic field shape descriptions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Magnetic field shape descriptions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Magnetic_quadrupole_moment.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="265" data-file-width="267" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Magnetic_quadrupole_moment.svg/220px-Magnetic_quadrupole_moment.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Magnetic_quadrupole_moment.svg/330px-Magnetic_quadrupole_moment.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Magnetic_quadrupole_moment.svg/440px-Magnetic_quadrupole_moment.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Magnetic_quadrupole_moment.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Schematic
         <a href="/wiki/Quadrupole_magnet" title="Quadrupole magnet">
          quadrupole magnet
         </a>
         ("
         <i>
          four-pole
         </i>
         ") magnetic field. There are four steel pole tips, two opposing magnetic north poles and two opposing magnetic south poles.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <ul>
       <li>
        An
        <i>
         azimuthal
        </i>
        magnetic field is one that runs east–west.
       </li>
       <li>
        A
        <i>
         meridional
        </i>
        magnetic field is one that runs north–south. In the
        <a href="/wiki/Solar_dynamo" title="Solar dynamo">
         solar dynamo
        </a>
        model of the Sun,
        <a href="/wiki/Differential_rotation" title="Differential rotation">
         differential rotation
        </a>
        of the solar plasma causes the meridional magnetic field to stretch into an azimuthal magnetic field, a process called the
        <i>
         omega-effect
        </i>
        . The reverse process is called the
        <i>
         alpha-effect
        </i>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
         <a href="#cite_note-56">
          [41]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        A
        <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_dipole" title="Magnetic dipole">
         <i>
          dipole
         </i>
         magnetic field
        </a>
        is one seen around a bar magnet or around a
        <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">
         charged
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" title="Elementary particle">
         elementary particle
        </a>
        with nonzero
        <a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">
         spin
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        A
        <a href="/wiki/Quadrupole_magnet" title="Quadrupole magnet">
         <i>
          quadrupole
         </i>
         magnetic field
        </a>
        is one seen, for example, between the poles of four bar magnets. The field strength grows linearly with the radial distance from its longitudinal axis.
       </li>
       <li>
        A
        <i>
         solenoidal
        </i>
        magnetic field is similar to a dipole magnetic field, except that a solid bar magnet is replaced by a hollow electromagnetic coil magnet.
       </li>
       <li>
        A
        <i>
         toroidal
        </i>
        magnetic field occurs in a doughnut-shaped coil, the electric current spiraling around the tube-like surface, and is found, for example, in a
        <a href="/wiki/Tokamak" title="Tokamak">
         tokamak
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        A
        <i>
         poloidal
        </i>
        magnetic field is generated by a current flowing in a ring, and is found, for example, in a
        <a href="/wiki/Tokamak" title="Tokamak">
         tokamak
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        A
        <i>
         radial
        </i>
        magnetic field is one in which field lines are directed from the center outwards, similar to the spokes in a bicycle wheel. An example can be found in a
        <a href="/wiki/Loudspeaker" title="Loudspeaker">
         loudspeaker
        </a>
        transducers (driver).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
         <a href="#cite_note-57">
          [42]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        A
        <i>
         helical
        </i>
        magnetic field is corkscrew-shaped, and sometimes seen in space plasmas such as the
        <a href="/wiki/Orion_Molecular_Cloud_Complex" title="Orion Molecular Cloud Complex">
         Orion Molecular Cloud
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
         <a href="#cite_note-58">
          [43]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_dipoles">
        Magnetic dipoles
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Magnetic dipoles">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:VFPt_dipole_magnetic3.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="600" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/VFPt_dipole_magnetic3.svg/220px-VFPt_dipole_magnetic3.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/VFPt_dipole_magnetic3.svg/330px-VFPt_dipole_magnetic3.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/VFPt_dipole_magnetic3.svg/440px-VFPt_dipole_magnetic3.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:VFPt_dipole_magnetic3.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Magnetic field lines around a ”magnetostatic dipole” pointing to the right.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_dipole" title="Magnetic dipole">
        Magnetic dipole
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Spin_magnetic_moment" title="Spin magnetic moment">
        Spin magnetic moment
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Micromagnetism" title="Micromagnetism">
        Micromagnetism
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The magnetic field of a magnetic dipole is depicted in the figure. From outside, the ideal magnetic dipole is identical to that of an ideal electric dipole of the same strength. Unlike the electric dipole, a magnetic dipole is properly modeled as a current loop having a current
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         I
        </i>
       </span>
       and an area
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         a
        </i>
       </span>
       . Such a current loop has a magnetic moment of:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle m=Ia,\,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mi>
               m
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mi>
               I
              </mi>
              <mi>
               a
              </mi>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
              <mspace width="thinmathspace">
              </mspace>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle m=Ia,\,}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="m=Ia, \," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/48419a141ca4a9bce6f7c177b2824e0c3dd4054e" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:8.574ex; height:2.509ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where the direction of
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         m
        </b>
       </span>
       is perpendicular to the area of the loop and depends on the direction of the current using the right-hand rule. An ideal magnetic dipole is modeled as a real magnetic dipole whose area
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         a
        </i>
       </span>
       has been reduced to zero and its current
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         I
        </i>
       </span>
       increased to infinity such that the product
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         m
        </i>
        =
        <i>
         Ia
        </i>
       </span>
       is finite. This model clarifies the connection between angular momentum and magnetic moment, which is the basis of the
       <a href="/wiki/Einstein%E2%80%93de_Haas_effect" title="Einstein–de Haas effect">
        Einstein–de Haas effect
       </a>
       <i>
        rotation by magnetization
       </i>
       and its inverse, the
       <a href="/wiki/Barnett_effect" title="Barnett effect">
        Barnett effect
       </a>
       or
       <i>
        magnetization by rotation
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graham_59-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Graham-59">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Rotating the loop faster (in the same direction) increases the current and therefore the magnetic moment, for example.
      </p>
      <p>
       It is sometimes useful to model the magnetic dipole similar to the electric dipole with two equal but opposite magnetic charges (one south the other north) separated by distance
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         d
        </i>
       </span>
       . This model produces an
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         H
        </b>
       </span>
       -field not a
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       -field. Such a model is deficient, though, both in that there are no magnetic charges and in that it obscures the link between electricity and magnetism. Further, as discussed above it fails to explain the inherent connection between
       <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum" title="Angular momentum">
        angular momentum
       </a>
       and magnetism.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Magnetic_monopole_.28hypothetical.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_monopole_(hypothetical)">
        Magnetic monopole (hypothetical)
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Magnetic monopole (hypothetical)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_monopole" title="Magnetic monopole">
        Magnetic monopole
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       A
       <i>
        magnetic monopole
       </i>
       is a hypothetical particle (or class of particles) that has, as its name suggests, only one magnetic pole (either a north pole or a south pole). In other words, it would possess a "magnetic charge" analogous to an electric charge. Magnetic field lines would start or end on magnetic monopoles, so if they exist, they would give exceptions to the rule that magnetic field lines neither start nor end.
      </p>
      <p>
       Modern interest in this concept stems from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-energy_physics" title="High-energy physics">
        particle theories
       </a>
       , notably
       <a href="/wiki/Grand_Unified_Theory" title="Grand Unified Theory">
        Grand Unified Theories
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Superstring_theory" title="Superstring theory">
        superstring theories
       </a>
       , that predict either the existence, or the possibility, of magnetic monopoles. These theories and others have inspired extensive efforts to search for monopoles. Despite these efforts, no magnetic monopole has been observed to date.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ex10_60-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ex10-60">
         [nb 16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In recent research, materials known as
       <a href="/wiki/Spin_ice" title="Spin ice">
        spin ices
       </a>
       can simulate monopoles, but do not contain actual monopoles.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-monopole_61-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-monopole-61">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MonopoleReview_62-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-MonopoleReview-62">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">
        See also
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: See also">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="General">
        General
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: General">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Magnetohydrodynamics" title="Magnetohydrodynamics">
         Magnetohydrodynamics
        </a>
        – the study of the dynamics of electrically conducting fluids
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_nanoparticles" title="Magnetic nanoparticles">
         Magnetic nanoparticles
        </a>
        – extremely small magnetic particles that are tens of atoms wide
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_reconnection" title="Magnetic reconnection">
         Magnetic reconnection
        </a>
        – an effect that causes
        <a href="/wiki/Solar_flare" title="Solar flare">
         solar flares
        </a>
        and auroras
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_potential" title="Magnetic potential">
         Magnetic potential
        </a>
        – the vector and
        <a href="/wiki/Scalar_potential" title="Scalar potential">
         scalar potential
        </a>
        representation of magnetism
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/SI_electromagnetism_units" title="SI electromagnetism units">
         SI electromagnetism units
        </a>
        – common units used in electromagnetism
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude_(magnetic_field)" title="Orders of magnitude (magnetic field)">
         Orders of magnitude (magnetic field)
        </a>
        – list of magnetic field sources and measurement devices from smallest magnetic fields to largest detected
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Upward_continuation" title="Upward continuation">
         Upward continuation
        </a>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematics">
        Mathematics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Mathematics">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_helicity" title="Magnetic helicity">
         Magnetic helicity
        </a>
        – extent to which a magnetic field wraps around itself
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">
        Applications
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Applications">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Dynamo_theory" title="Dynamo theory">
         Dynamo theory
        </a>
        – a proposed mechanism for the creation of the Earth's magnetic field
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Helmholtz_coil" title="Helmholtz coil">
         Helmholtz coil
        </a>
        – a device for producing a region of nearly uniform magnetic field
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field_viewing_film" title="Magnetic field viewing film">
         Magnetic field viewing film
        </a>
        – Film used to view the magnetic field of an area
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Maxwell_coil" title="Maxwell coil">
         Maxwell coil
        </a>
        – a device for producing a large volume of an almost constant magnetic field
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Stellar_magnetic_field" title="Stellar magnetic field">
         Stellar magnetic field
        </a>
        – a discussion of the magnetic field of stars
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Teltron_tube" title="Teltron tube">
         Teltron tube
        </a>
        – device used to display an electron beam and demonstrates effect of electric and magnetic fields on moving charges
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">
        Notes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_field&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Notes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns">
       <ol class="references">
        <li id="cite_note-3">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-3">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          His
          <i>
           Epistola Petri Peregrini de Maricourt ad Sygerum de Foucaucourt Militem de Magnete
          </i>
          , which is often shortened to
          <i>
           Epistola de magnete
          </i>
          , is dated 1269 C.E.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-9">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-9">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          From the outside, the field of a dipole of magnetic charge has exactly the same form as a current loop when both are sufficiently small. Therefore, the two models differ only for magnetism inside magnetic material.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-17">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-17">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          The letters B and H were originally chosen by Maxwell in his
          <i>
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treatise_on_Electricity_and_Magnetism" title="Treatise on Electricity and Magnetism">
            Treatise on Electricity and Magnetism
           </a>
          </i>
          (Vol. II, pp. 236–237). For many quantities, he simply started choosing letters from the beginning of the alphabet. See
          <cite class="citation journal">
           Ralph Baierlein (2000). "Answer to Question #73. S is for entropy, Q is for charge".
           <i>
            American Journal of Physics
           </i>
           .
           <b>
            68
           </b>
           (8): 691.
           <a href="/wiki/Bibcode" title="Bibcode">
            Bibcode
           </a>
           :
           <a class="external text" href="http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2000AmJPh..68..691B" rel="nofollow">
            2000AmJPh..68..691B
           </a>
           .
           <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">
            doi
           </a>
           :
           <a class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1119%2F1.19524" rel="nofollow">
            10.1119/1.19524
           </a>
           .
          </cite>
          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=American+Journal+of+Physics&amp;rft.atitle=Answer+to+Question+%2373.+S+is+for+entropy%2C+Q+is+for+charge&amp;rft.volume=68&amp;rft.issue=8&amp;rft.pages=691&amp;rft.date=2000&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1119%2F1.19524&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F2000AmJPh..68..691B&amp;rft.au=Ralph+Baierlein&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMagnetic+field">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-ex03-18">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-ex03_18-0">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <a href="/wiki/Edward_Mills_Purcell" title="Edward Mills Purcell">
           Edward Purcell
          </a>
          , in Electricity and Magnetism, McGraw-Hill, 1963, writes,
          <i>
           Even some modern writers who treat
           <span class="texhtml">
            <b>
             B
            </b>
           </span>
           as the primary field feel obliged to call it the magnetic induction because the name magnetic field was historically preempted by
           <span class="texhtml">
            <b>
             H
            </b>
           </span>
           . This seems clumsy and pedantic. If you go into the laboratory and ask a physicist what causes the pion trajectories in his bubble chamber to curve, he'll probably answer "magnetic field", not "magnetic induction." You will seldom hear a geophysicist refer to the Earth's magnetic induction, or an astrophysicist talk about the magnetic induction of the galaxy. We propose to keep on calling
           <span class="texhtml">
            <b>
             B
            </b>
           </span>
           the magnetic field. As for
           <span class="texhtml">
            <b>
             H
            </b>
           </span>
           , although other names have been invented for it, we shall call it "the field
           <span class="texhtml">
            <b>
             H
            </b>
           </span>
           " or even "the magnetic field
           <span class="texhtml">
            <b>
             H
            </b>
           </span>
           ."
          </i>
          In a similar vein,
          <cite class="citation book">
           M Gerloch (1983).
           <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/?id=Ovo8AAAAIAAJ&amp;pg=PA110" rel="nofollow">
            <i>
             Magnetism and Ligand-field Analysis
            </i>
           </a>
           . Cambridge University Press. p. 110.
           <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
            ISBN
           </a>
           <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-521-24939-2" title="Special:BookSources/0-521-24939-2">
            0-521-24939-2
           </a>
           .
          </cite>
          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Magnetism+and+Ligand-field+Analysis&amp;rft.pages=110&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft.date=1983&amp;rft.isbn=0-521-24939-2&amp;rft.au=M+Gerloch&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2F%3Fid%3DOvo8AAAAIAAJ%26pg%3DPA110&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMagnetic+field">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
          says: "So we may think of both
          <span class="texhtml">
           <b>
            B
           </b>
          </span>
          and
          <span class="texhtml">
           <b>
            H
           </b>
          </span>
          as magnetic fields, but drop the word 'magnetic' from
          <span class="texhtml">
           <b>
            H
           </b>
          </span>
          so as to maintain the distinction ... As Purcell points out, 'it is only the names that give trouble, not the symbols'."
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-19">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-19">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          This can be seen from the magnetic part of the Lorentz force law
          <span class="texhtml">
           <i>
            F
           </i>
           =
           <i>
            qvB
           </i>
           sinθ
          </span>
          .
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-ex07-25">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-ex07_25-0">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          The use of iron filings to display a field presents something of an exception to this picture; the filings alter the magnetic field so that it is much larger along the "lines" of iron, due to the large
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_permeability" title="Magnetic permeability">
           permeability
          </a>
          of iron relative to air.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-ex05-26">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-ex05_26-0">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Here 'small' means that the observer is sufficiently far away that it can be treated as being infinitesimally small. 'Larger' magnets need to include more complicated terms in the expression and depend on the entire geometry of the magnet not just
          <span class="texhtml">
           <b>
            m
           </b>
          </span>
          .
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-27">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-27">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Magnetic field lines may also wrap around and around without closing but also without ending. These more complicated non-closing non-ending magnetic field lines are moot, though, since the magnetic field of objects that produce them are calculated by adding the magnetic fields of 'elementary parts' having magnetic field lines that do form closed curves or extend to infinity.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-ex08-28">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-ex08_28-0">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          To see that this must be true imagine placing a compass inside a magnet. There, the north pole of the compass points toward the north pole of the magnet since magnets stacked on each other point in the same direction.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-ex09-29">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-ex09_29-0">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          As discussed above, magnetic field lines are primarily a conceptual tool used to represent the mathematics behind magnetic fields. The total 'number' of field lines is dependent on how the field lines are drawn. In practice, integral equations such as the one that follows in the main text are used instead.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-ex04-30">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-ex04_30-0">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Either
          <span class="texhtml">
           <b>
            B
           </b>
          </span>
          or
          <span class="texhtml">
           <b>
            H
           </b>
          </span>
          may be used for the magnetic field outside the magnet.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-ex12-33">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-ex12_33-0">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          In practice, the Biot–Savart law and other laws of magnetostatics are often used even when a current change in time, as long as it does not change too quickly. It is often used, for instance, for standard household currents, which oscillate sixty times per second.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-ex13-36">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-ex13_36-0">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          The Biot–Savart law contains the additional restriction (boundary condition) that the B-field must go to zero fast enough at infinity. It also depends on the divergence of
          <span class="texhtml">
           <b>
            B
           </b>
          </span>
          being zero, which is always valid. (There are no magnetic charges.)
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-41">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-41">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          A third term is needed for changing electric fields and polarization currents; this displacement current term is covered in Maxwell's equations below.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-ex14-48">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-ex14_48-0">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">

          <span class="texhtml">
           <b>
            E
           </b>
          </span>
          and magnetic fields can be written as:

              <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
               {\displaystyle \textstyle =-{\frac {d}{dt}}\iint _{\Sigma (t)}d{\boldsymbol {A}}\cdot \mathbf {B} (\mathbf {r} ,\ t),}
              </annotation>
             </semantics>
            </math>
           </span>
           <img alt="\textstyle=-\frac {d} {dt}  \iint_{\Sigma (t)} d \boldsymbol {A} \cdot \mathbf {B}(\mathbf{r},\ t)," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9fc6bf7dc4f07205d0e954bbcb1f37851ef50aa5" style="vertical-align: -1.505ex; width:25.348ex; height:4.009ex;"/>
          </span>
          where
          <span class="texhtml">
           <b>
            ∂Σ
           </b>
           (
           <i>
            t
           </i>
           )
          </span>
          is the moving closed path bounding the moving surface
          <span class="texhtml">
           <b>
            Σ
           </b>
           (
           <i>
            t
           </i>
           )
          </span>
          , and
          <span class="texhtml">
           d
           <b>
            A
           </b>
          </span>
          is an element of surface area of
          <span class="texhtml">
           <b>
            Σ
           </b>
           (
           <i>
            t
           </i>
           )
          </span>
          . The first integral calculates the work done moving a charge a distance
          <span class="texhtml">
           d
           <b>
            ℓ
           </b>
          </span>
          based upon the Lorentz force law. In the case where the bounding surface is stationary, the
          <a href="/wiki/Kelvin%E2%80%93Stokes_theorem" title="Kelvin–Stokes theorem">
           Kelvin–Stokes theorem
          </a>
          can be used to show this equation is equivalent to the Maxwell–Faraday equation.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-ex10-60">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-ex10_60-0">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Two experiments produced candidate events that were initially interpreted as monopoles, but these are now considered inconclusive. For details and references, see
          <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_monopole" title="Magnetic monopole">
           magnetic monopole
          </a>
          .
